The author of this book says: When I had completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of medical science in its completeness, and had reached the utmost in its clarity and explanation, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And only a few outlines of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been copied by hands and marred by mistakes and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its usefulness has been lost.
Therefore I saw fit to revive it and compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this is a means of increasing clarity and of facilitating what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled practitioner in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must beforehand practice in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and knows the bones, nerves, muscles, their number and origins, the pulsating and non-pulsating vessels, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned something of this in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not learned in what we have mentioned concerning anatomy will inevitably fall into error, and kill people thereby, as I have often witnessed among those who have imagined themselves to be skilled in this science and have claimed it without knowledge or practice.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a porcine tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the bladder; the stone was large, so he was reckless and removed it with a piece of the bladder's substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been summoned to remove it; and I saw from the size of the stone and the state of the patient that I could have done it.
And I saw another physician who earned his living from some of the brothel keepers of our town through medicine.
Then a black boy came to him who had a fracture in his leg near the heel, along with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, quickly bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and left no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he left him to his desires, and left him for days and ordered him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was near death.
Then I was summoned to him, and I quickly untied the bandage, so he found relief and was freed from his pains; except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb, and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after days until the affliction of its owner became great.  That is because if a cancer is purely of black humor, then it should not be treated with iron at all.
Except it be in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my sons, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety, and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned, in every place it comes in this book, against the operation which contains danger and fear; therefore you should avoid and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to speak and criticize, take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
And purify yourselves from that which you fear might cast doubt on your religion and your worldly life, for that is more lasting for your reputation and higher in this world and the next for your status.
For Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become bad physicians.
This chapter is divided into three sections.
The first section.
On cauterization with fire and cauterization with strong medicine, arranged sequentially from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cautery irons and everything needed for the procedure.
And the second section.
On incision, puncturing, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged sequentially with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third section.
On setting and reduction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, arranged sequentially from head to foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first section.
On cauterization.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
And I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization is a lengthy discussion, a precise science, and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened their speech for fear of lengthening.
And I say that cauterization generally benefits every bad temperament that is with a substance and without a substance, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without substance and the dry temperament without substance.
As for the dry temperament with a substance, they differed in it. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry medicine. And the one who says the opposite says that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say what he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has practiced and trained in the field of cauterization to a great extent. And he has observed the difference in people's temperaments and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear for you from them, especially the cold and moist diseases.  All the physicians have agreed on them, and they have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my sons, that among the secrets of treatment is cauterization with fire, and its advantage over cauterization with a burning drug, because fire is a simple element whose effect does not extend beyond the member that is cauterized, and it does not harm another connected member except slightly.
And cauterization with a burning drug may extend its effect to other members, and it may cause a disease in the member that is difficult to treat, and it may even kill.  Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one exceeds.
And this has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding of the realities of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse.  Were it not that it is unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of diseases, with philosophical, demonstrative speech too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at any time, because the harm resulting from the time is absorbed by the benefit brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, urgent, necessary pains that do not tolerate delay, because of the fear that what follows from them might be a calamity greater than the slight harm caused by the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a disease will never return, and they make it a guarantee.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is only like medicine which alters the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cauterization is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and the gathering of humors in his body, and his neglect of himself in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like, for it is possible that that pain may not return, and this is in the least.
And as for the saying of the common people also that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a correct saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment is beneficial by medicine or otherwise after cauterization has occurred, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cauterization as the last thing, and it works; from here it is said that cauterization is the last of medicine. Not in the sense that the common people and many ignorant physicians went to.
And the ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate; and this is not absolutely the case.
Because I have tried it and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and more excellent than iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery in the fire, made of gold, you cannot determine when it is heated to the degree you want, due to the redness of the gold.
And because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and is spoiled; thus the craftsman is troubled by this. Therefore, cauterization with iron is, for us, quicker and closer to the correct practice, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections that I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
The first chapter.
On cauterizing the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the predominance of moisture and cold on the brain, which are the cause of headache, frequent colds from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain. And in short, for every disease that results from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, aphasia, and similar diseases.
The manner of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habit of the patient.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your hand on the root of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger reaches, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, whose image is this.
Expose the bone the size of a clove or the size of a carob seed. Then lift your hand, or if it cools, repeat with the same or a different instrument. Until you see what I have described of the bone. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, soak a cotton swab in it, apply it to the area, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter and leave it there until the scab from the cautery is gone. Then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone thickness is burned, then removing the burned bone, and then treating it.
Others said that one should exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone like a carat or a small flake.  They claimed that this vents the head's vapors from that place, and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals. I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and by way of risk, and in my opinion leaving it is better and safer.  For if it is done, the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have witnessed in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and it is what I use.  Act accordingly, and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and it persists in the patient, and poultices, gargles, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization as we described has been performed and none of this has helped, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him in the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this and do not expose the bone, for the patient will find this very painful, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head. I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the temples and the back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head. This is its form.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head along with a headache and the pain extends to the eye, then purge the patient with medicines that purify the head and use all the treatments that I mentioned in the divisions of diseases.  If that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it, and cut off its ends from both sides. Then incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin that fits the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with a bandage, and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I gave in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery, which is of this form:
It is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail, with some perforation and a small protrusion in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn of the thickness of the skin should be about half of it. Then lift your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this chronic migraine, especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterizing chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with what we have mentioned of the aforementioned treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not work, and you see a cause that cannot be treated with what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire. Then it is necessary to heat the knife-like cautery until it glows white after you have marked the painful area with a line half a fingerbreadth long or thereabouts, and you should move your hand once while pulling it so as to cut the artery and reach towards the bone.
Except that you should be cautious of touching the jaw, which moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it, and thus cause spasms.
And be cautious and careful about the bleeding from the artery that you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and is not a skilled and experienced person; leaving the operation is preferable.
And the treatment of bleeding that occurs from an artery will be mentioned in its proper place in the book, God willing, if you see a condition that this cautery cannot manage.
And you saw the patient's body able to withstand it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery that we mentioned in the chapter on artery ligation with the double-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
In cauterizing earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxatives and other treatments that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it with points around the entire ear, as it circles, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little distance from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cauterization is that type which arises from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
And one should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with ointments, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has not been effective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, for the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
And the image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cauterization opposite the upper part of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, but it is a little gentler, as you see. And the knife should have a little more thickness, then treat the area with what has been mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
In cauterizing the chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned and your treatment is not effective and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head as previously described.
The prescription for the cautery is as previously mentioned.  It may also be cauterized at the mouth of the stomach, which will be more effective, then treat it as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is caused by phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his whole head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, as described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a kind of cauterization.
Do this in the same order I mentioned there; if he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, cauterize three times at the back of his head, in a row from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger.  Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle area.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize the temples, then treat him until he recovers. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
Chapter Nine.
Cauterization for paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you begin by purifying the head with purgatives and as we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one at the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in a case of general body relaxation, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and intractable, then cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of his back. And carry the cauterization to the point of burning most of the skin, then lift your hand, then treat him as previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing. And let the cautery be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
Cauterization for Epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  It is necessary to first purify his brain with strong purgatives and with the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to tolerate medication.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then let him use gargles and masticatories that purify the brain for many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then his head should be shaved, and then one cauterization should be performed in the middle of the head, as previously described, and another cauterization at the back of the head. And one cauterization on each temple; if the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, then perform the cauterizations described for the patient with paralysis and relaxation of the body, on the vertebrae of the neck and vertebrae of the back. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then perform the cauterizations we mentioned for the paralytic.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined towards black bile and the patient's body is moist, then purge what cleanses his brain as was previously explained in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head, and the patient sits cross-legged holding it from each side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a fire to a moderate temperature, as much as the finger can tolerate when inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once a week along with all other good treatment until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding your hand to the cautery iron, but rather by fumigation, for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing the flow of water in the eye.
If the onset of the flow of water in the eye becomes apparent to you through the signs mentioned in the classification, then act quickly and purge the patient of what pollutes his head, protect him from all moisture, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize him in the middle of the head, then cauterize him twice on the temples if the onset of the flow of water is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the temples, and guard against the bleeding of blood.
If you see something of it, cut it on the spot with any treatment possible to you, and we will come to the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And he may be cauterized in the nape of the neck under the two bones with two intense cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
In cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and they are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that this is from cold, thick, phlegmatic superfluities, then cauterize it with the cautery which I described in its place at the beginning of the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head and two cauterizations on the temples and two cauterizations in the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need to increase [the cauterizations], then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the corner of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
In cauterizing the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment does not work, then act quickly and purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with the olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, a little under the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped [cautery iron].
Chapter Fifteen.
In cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, then cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, then cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, and keep a distance from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be along the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate your hand with the cautery, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery iron is like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins turn inward so that the eye is inverted, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a cauterizing drug. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If you cauterize it while they are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. Then when they have grown and become even, place the patient’s head in your lap, and then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of an ivy leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron; this is its shape.
Then cauterize according to the mark you made, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the ivy leaf, is entirely burned, especially its surface. A sign of the correctness of your work is that you see the eyelid curled and the hair raised from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days.  Then apply cotton soaked in butter to it until the eschar separates. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization on that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a caustic medicine, you should instruct the patient to let the margins grow until they are long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an ivy leaf. Then take of the known soap and of unslaked lime, of each one a drachm or so, and grind them together very finely. Do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper you made in the shape of an ivy leaf and place it on one or both eyelids. Place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on top of the medicine and hold it down gently, moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look; if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting, then leave it. Otherwise, apply more of the medicine to the place where the medicine did not take effect and did not blacken, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized. Then apply cotton with butter to it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm-tree ointment or the like until it heals. When working, you should be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and there is a fear of something in the eye, [proceed as follows].
Then reapply the medicine to that relaxed part of the eyelid in the same way as you did initially, then treat it until it heals.  This is the image of an ivy leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so adjust your cauterization accordingly. The correct method is not hidden from anyone experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the polyp that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the polyp with what we mentioned in the classification of diseases and your treatment is ineffective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and have an attendant hold his head firmly so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then place a cotton ball moistened with egg white or the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then heat the cautery iron, which is of this form.
It should be hollow, in the shape of a tube, like a bird's quill, at one end, which is the cauterizing end.  If you wish, it can be perforated at the other end, or if you wish, it can be solid like an awl, although the hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the polyp if it is open and remove its contents and dry it, or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus. Then immediately apply the very hot cautery iron, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose during cauterization, lest your hand waver or the patient become anxious and the cautery iron fall into the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter to it and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and remove it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a polyp, mentioned by some of the ancients, is to approach the location of the polyp, incise it, and then place a thin cone in the incision itself.  This is its image.
Then pour into it an amount equal to the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold the cone firmly and securely; and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye. It is advisable to place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the site of the polyp and heal it wonderfully. If the polyp heals with the cauterization and treatment that we have mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal perforation and return the polyp to the nasal passage, as will be described in its more appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a harelip, especially in the lips of children; for it often occurs. If you treat this cleft with what we have mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this shape.
Its cavity should be as thin as a knife. Then apply it, hot and quickly, to the cleft itself, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with the qiروطى until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing fistulas occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the flow of pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with the treatment we have mentioned, until it heals, God willing.
If the discharge ceases and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the decayed bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On the loosening of the molars and gums.
If the gums are loosened by moisture and the molars are mobile, and you have treated them with medication without success, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery iron, whose form will be shown later, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then return the cautery iron repeatedly, as much as you want, then the patient fills his mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and then spits it out; for the mobile tooth will become firm, and the loosened gum will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry.
Chapter twenty-one.
In cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and treatment with medication has not been effective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, you take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then you dip it in the boiled butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the strength of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  And if you wish, you may dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, you take a brass tube or an iron tube, and it should have some thickness in its body so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron, the image of which is shown, and place it on the tooth itself, and hold your hand until the cautery iron cools. Do this several times.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next day. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, and according to what is possible. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If the fistulas are due to phlegm and cold humors and do not yield to ripening by medications, and you desire a quick ripening, then heat the hollow cautery iron, the image of which is this, which is perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits at cauterization from the other end. Place it, protected, on the same swelling once and twice if you need to do so until you reach the depth of the swelling. If the swelling is small, then make the cautery iron to the size of the swelling.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail over the windpipe, especially if that is with coldness of temperament, then it is necessary that the patient first be purged with laxative medicines. Then cauterize a cautery in the hollow of the throat at the root of the gullet in the lower place.
Beware of reaching the gullet with the cautery, and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then cauterize another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, then cauterize two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped, in the manner previously described. And if you wish, let your cauterization be by puncturing with the cautery called the “point,” the image of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache; and let the points number from thirty points or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers.  You may also make a cautery with three branches in this manner, and it hastens cauterization because you cauterize three cauteries at once with it. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain in place when it is reduced from its dislocation so that it becomes habitually dislocated and then dislocates at the slightest movement it is subjected to, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his sound side, and then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery with two points, which is of this form.
Then cauterize with it the skin until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations. It may be cauterized with a cautery with three points, so its shape is then six cauterizations, and the points should be on the level of a rosebud.
And this is the image of the cautery with three points.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations are eight. Then apply to the cauterizations crushed balls with salt, and then the patient should be kept quiet and not move the limb for a while until it is strong. And if the dislocation is upwards, which is rarely the case,
cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-like cauterization, or many cauterizations as punctures; for the joint will strengthen and the moisture will leave and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If cold and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, to the extent that it disrupts its temperament and  catarrhs to it increase, and it is treated with various treatments but to no avail, then the patient should lie on his back, extending his legs and arms, then cauterize with three cauterizations. One cauterization below the clavicle, the size of a finger, with a nail-like cautery, and two cauterizations lower down on either side of the first cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and farther apart so that they do not join if they suppurate, and the depth of the cauterization should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery with which you cauterize this single cauterization should be circular, in this manner.
And you may cauterize the stomach by puncturing it for someone who is afraid of this cauterization; and that is that you mark points on the stomach to the extent that you desire with ink, then cauterize them with a punctate cautery, then treat it with the treatment which was described until it heals.
Chapter twenty-seven.
In the cold cauterization of the liver.
If there appears from the liver a pain due to coldness and dampness, or due to a subtle wind, until it has departed from its natural temperament an excessive departure, and the patient is treated with what we mentioned in the division, and that does not work. Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations are marked with ink in this shape and this precise amount on the liver, below the ribs where the elbow of a person ends.
And after each cauterization will be a cauterization of the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization will be along the body straight, and do not press down gently with the cautery iron.
And let the amount you burn of the thickness of the skin be half of it, no more. And let the patient be standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, let him be lying down with his legs extended and his arms raised. And this is the image of the cautery iron.
And you may be able to cauterize these cauterizations with the knife-cautery if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be cautious lest you exceed in the cauterization and burn all the skin and burn the abdomen and reach the intestines, for the skin there is thin; so know this.
Chapter twenty-eight.
In cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you wish to know if that swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will find heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be severe sharpness with the pain, and you see that physicians have been unable to treat it, then the patient should lie on his back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a needle, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose experience in the art of medicine is long and who has treated these diseases many times through experience; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with rhizomes of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this manner: that you take one long, dry rhizome of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and it should be as thick as a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then make one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugular veins, slightly inclined toward the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backward, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations on the back: one between the shoulder blades and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulder blades.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be on the surface of the skin superficially. Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used an iron cautery like a spatula; they would heat it and insert it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the pus is evacuated as we mentioned in liver tumors.  In this method of cauterization there is danger; either the patient dies immediately or a fistula forms at the site which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the spleen disease with what we mentioned in the division and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all correct. One is to cauterize it three or four times in a row along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose form we have described, and between each cauterization and the next is the width of a finger or a little more.  The type of cautery is the same as we mentioned in cauterizing the liver.  Do not deepen your hand with the cautery, and the patient's position is lying on his back.
The other way is in the flank, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and you raise the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left flank ends, and your raising of the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations are along the body.
Then you insert the two cautery irons, very carefully protected, until you pierce the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery iron, and the cauterizations will be four. And if you wish, you can cauterize with the other cautery iron with three points.
Then you treat the place of cauterization after you leave it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned previously.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascites specifically, if you treat the patient with dropsy using the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two meanings for him, so that healing will hasten to him. The shape of the cauterizing iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its image has preceded. That with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the hollow between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cauterizing iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will exude, and the cauterizing iron is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization is to be with the sharp end of the cauterizing iron, and the cauterization is to be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below it towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water may exude from it, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter thirty-three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, so that it weakens the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see the patient able to withstand cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As was previously mentioned in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron, and four cauterizations around the navel gently with the mild nail-shaped cautery iron, and one or two cauterizations on the cotton above the coccyx.
If you see that the dampness is abundant and the patient is able to withstand it, then cauterize him on the pubis and cauterize him on each loin, and perhaps you can add two small cauterizations on the stomach near the large one; for this is a successful treatment whose benefit is not missed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic hemorrhoids, many or one, in the anus, and they are of thick, cold humors or putrid moisture, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the classification but the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, a little below the coccyx, in a triangular shape.
And a cauterization below the navel by the length of two fingers; and if you estimate that his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as mentioned before, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for a while, then treat him until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and persevere until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with suitable ointments until they heal. And if you cauterize it with a large cautery on cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and is afraid of it, then perhaps he will heal with cauterization. If a fistula occurs and pus and corrupt humors flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery that resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and the extent to which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads to the substance of the bladder or to the substance of the intestine.
Therefore, be careful of all these places. You only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you estimate that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place closes and the materials cease from it and it remains like that for a time, then know that it has completely healed.
And if the materials do not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that there is a decayed bone in its depth, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is diminished because of that, then it is necessary to cauterize it on the loins, over the kidneys themselves. Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery that was previously mentioned.  And perhaps cauterize a third time in the same place, so that there are three cauterizations in a row, which will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
The distance after cauterization on each side should be the length of a thumb joint. And cauterize one cauterization in the lower back, or two if necessary. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If a substance from cold and dampness occurs in the uterus, and because of that it is prevented from conception and its temperament is corrupted and its functions are impaired, or pain occurs in it upon the arrival of menses, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One cauterization on the cotton in the lower back, or two, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous moist substances may settle in the hip joint, becoming the cause of its dislocation. Its sign is that the leg is longer than the other when they are measured against each other, and you find emptiness in the place of the dislocation.  Then it is necessary to cauterize the patient on the hip joint itself with a semi-circular cautery.
After you have drawn with ink around the joint as it rotates, so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle, and the cautery whose image is presented in cauterizing the stomach. If you do not have this cautery, then cauterize with three cauterizations using the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach the entire thickness of the skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, and the cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the section, and the treatment has not been effective, and this has lasted for some time, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with hellebore or prickly pear seed, or something similar.
Then shave it and apply the cautery in two ways: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire. Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  A cauterization may also be made in the middle, on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations. Or, if you wish, cauterize it in a circle, as previously mentioned in the cauterization for hip dislocation, with a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two places on the thigh where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the heel, four fingers towards the lateral side, slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or lasting harm.
And I have witnessed one and another who was cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive, so the leg became gangrenous until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the entire leg was corrupted. Then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned concerning the cauterization of the hip a cauterization of this type:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter being half a span, and its mouth the thickness of a date pit, or slightly less; and within this cup is another cup, and a third cup.
The distance between each cup is the width of a thumb joint; the cups are open on both sides; their height is about a joint or two joints; and a handle of firmly attached iron is made for them.  This is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip, the patient leaning on the sound side. Three round cauterizations are performed at once. Then he is left for three days, and the wound is dressed with butter, leaving the wound open for some days.
Many then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book says: We rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because of the scarcity of those who can endure it; however, it is among the best cauterizations for those who endure it and have the affected area treated with it.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is done by making two cup-like things, similar to the rings through which the hinges of a door pass, from copper or iron.
Their rims are about two finger joints high, and the distance between them is the width of a finger; they are open at the bottom and open at the top, and they are held together in this manner.
Then you lower it onto the hip joint, with the patient lying on his sound side, and you firmly hold your hand. Then you pour between the circles some caustic water, which has been warmed a little over the fire. You hold it for about an hour, and the patient endures its stinging and burning, for he feels a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you lift the instrument with your hand and wipe the caustic water from the entire hip with fresh water. Then you leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears. You then leave it for some days, so that pus may drain from it; for that is more effective.
Then you treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, you make a mold for it which holds the water, in the same way as you did for the hip.  This is the description of the caustic water, which I have already described in the chapter on the preparation of drugs.
Take one part of potash and one part of unslaked lime, crush them, and put them in a new pot which has a small hole pierced in its bottom, large enough for a probe to enter.  Place another glazed pot beneath the bottom of the pot.
Pour fresh water over the potash and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after having pressed them well together with your hand. Leave the pot until the caustic water drips into the glazed pot below. Then collect all that water, and pour it onto more fresh lime and potash.
Then it will be extremely potent, useful in many medical procedures and in cauterizing various members, because it acts just like fire itself.  There are other drugs which cauterize the hip as well, such as thapsia, honey of henbane, and lime with soap, mixed together.
Galen recounted a story from the ancients about a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, and greatly emphasized its importance, claiming that no other treatment was needed and that it cured in a single application.
So much so that one might enter the bath carried and leave cured.  This is done by taking fresh green verdigris—if fresh is unavailable, then use recently dried verdigris—finely pulverizing it with some fat, and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg, or to the thigh.
It is bandaged and left for three hours, or however long the patient feels the burning sensation subsiding. Then he is brought into the bath. When his body is damp, he is placed in the tub.  Then the pain will go away, and he will be healed, God willing. If it is not cured, repeat the application after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that one should not cauterize with these drugs unless the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization is done in this way: wool is soaked in old oil and placed on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take a dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another one and continue doing this until sensation reaches from the middle of the arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called goat-hair cauterization.
Chapter forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Back pain may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It is caused by the effusion of a cold, moist substance, and cauterization is only applied in this type alone.
That which is caused by the effusion of a cold substance: after the patient has vomited with hellebore and the like, he should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the location with ink.  In each row, five cauterizations or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery; or if you wish, cauterize three or four times with a medium-sized nail-shaped cautery, in this manner.
Chapter eighty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment frequently affects young children.  The sign of its beginning in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and movement, and you will find at the end of the vertebrae of his back a bead that has protruded and stands out from the other beads.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery iron in this manner. Let the cauterization take hold on each side of the vertebra equally.  And if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed cautery iron in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another. Then treat the area until it heals as we have mentioned.
And be careful not to use cauterization in a hump that is from a spasm of the nerve.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are from cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, then it is customary for physicians to call this gout specifically.
If cold-caused gout is treated with the kinds of treatments we mentioned in the classification and the pains do not go away, then cauterization will remove them.  This is done by cauterizing around the joints of the legs with many cauterizations after purgation, and the cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the surface of the leg, do so with a pointed cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees or to the other joints—and this often happens—cauterize each knee with three or four cauterizations on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your hand with the cautery; rather, it should be about the thickness of the skin only. If the pains ascend to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned of cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, cauterize each joint with a point and the metacarpus. If the pains ascend after days to the elbows or to the shoulders,
cauterize them on each side. The patient should not be without good management and the taking of medicines, for if he improves his diet and purges the phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some intestine and fat descend to the testicle, and this is recent and beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from eating that day and to use laxatives that will move the stool from his bowel.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should tell him to hold his breath until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger. Then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its extremities toward the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then a servant should place his hand on the place so that the intestine does not protrude.  And you have spread the patient's legs apart and placed a cushion under him, and another servant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be firm and straight, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it did not reach the bone the first time.
And take care to prevent the intestine from protruding while you cauterize, lest you burn it, which will cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not be successful. And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scabs from the fire are gone. Then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is healed.
And you should make his food during his treatment such as softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. And let him reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
For if this procedure is used in this way, he will recover completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. And as for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its beginning stages, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize a circular area around it to the extent of the hernia. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it. Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients have mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the top of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you place a single nail-like cautery in the middle, the first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some member due to a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time, and treatment with medicine does not work, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery.  The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, fine. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization, because it is the nature of these pains to spread from the member to what is near it. Therefore, it is necessary to pursue them with cauterization until the patient is cured.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, look: if the leprosy is incipient and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the section, and it has not subsided nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will overcome the whole temperament of the patient, then cauterize the head five times. One in the middle of the head as is customary, the second below it towards the forehead at the hairline, two on the temples, and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  And be thorough with the cauterization, a little, until it slightly affects the bone.
And scales will be shed from it, so that the thick vapors may easily escape.  Cauterize also the spleen in the same way as before.  But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and has appeared evidently, then it is necessary to cauterize with these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on the cheeks, two cauterizations on the cervical vertebrae, six on the dorsal vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine, and another above it in the same region, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the breasts, and cauterize each joint of the fingers and toes, and each heel, and the forearms.
And take care of the nerve which is at the back of the heels, lest you burn them.  And a cauterization may be made on the pubic bone, and another on the pit of the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you make, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of cauterization as the healthy person does, because his body is numb. And the cauteries should be large and small according to the organs and joints, as described in the properties of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with crushed acorns mixed with honey and other treatment until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medication, ointments, and bandages, but does not heal, cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the bone of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep and penetrate the thickness of the skin slightly.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Some numbness that affects the hand and foot may be cauterized at the vertebrae of the spine at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, and the numbness will disappear.
And let no one undertake this except one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy is longstanding and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the skin's thickness, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose ointment,  lamb's tongue, and pigeon's blood or the blood of a sparrowhawk; one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows.  Some learned men have mentioned cauterizing it intensely in its center, but I do not see that, because I expect that it will ulcerate, and I have witnessed that many times.
Therefore, it is correct to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of the fistula.
If a fistula occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the strength of its causative agent, or because of the age of the patient if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the fistula, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations, punctually.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.  If you wish to slow it with cauterization, use a cautery iron of this form.
And insert it into the middle of the fistula until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the flow of pus is easier. Then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is a corruption that spreads in the member and consumes it, as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, then use many small and large, nail-like cauteries according to what is suitable for that place where the ulcer is.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing at all remains. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas ground sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy and without corruption, then do nothing. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on whatever remains of the corrupted areas. And you may also treat the ulcer with a caustic medication.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have already mentioned its treatment with caustic medication in the classification, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the man.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with caustic water.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it encircles the nail on all sides, and it should have a thin edge. Then you apply it hot to the nail, and you turn your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, it will suppurate, then you dress it with pounded wild lettuce mixed with salt, and you leave the dressing on it overnight, then it will be removed from its roots. Then you treat the wound with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
And it often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet.  Then you should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's feather, and place it over the nail or wart, then pour a little caustic water into the tube.
And hold your hand while turning it, with a little movement of your hand, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the caustic water finds a way to penetrate to the root of the nail.  Let the patient endure the sting of the caustic water for a little while.
Then leave it, for the nail will be removed with its roots.  Do this one by one until you have removed all of them from the body, then treat the places after they are removed with ointments that grow flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing the falling [hair/disease].
If someone suffers from falling hair due to a cold in the nerves, or from a quartan fever or something else, then you should cauterize him four or five times on the vertebrae of the back, with one cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on his chest. And on his stomach a cauterization with the olive cautery, for the falling [hair/disease] will be calmed and the cold illness will quickly mature.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing boils that occur on the body.
Ugly boils may erupt on the body, resulting from cold, thick, and corrupt materials. At their first appearance, each boil should be gently cauterized on its head with a sharpened stick whose tip has been ignited in the fire, or with a long piece of ziraund, or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And the abscesses may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that the effective cause will not increase and will dissipate, and the patient will recover from it; however, this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Frequently, bleeding occurs from an artery that has been severed during a wound inflicted from the outside, or during the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and the like, and its severance is difficult.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly so that the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing comes out of it.  Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, according to the wound and the location where the artery has ruptured, and apply the cautery to the same vessel after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, upon removing your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery is extinguished, quickly take another cautery from those in the prepared fire, and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
And be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest another affliction befall the patient. And know that if an artery bleeds, it cannot be severed, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. For if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or it may be tightly bound with threads; or medications that stop bleeding may be applied to it, and tightly bound with bandages.
And as for anyone who attempts to sever it with a ligature, or by tightening with cloths, or applying burning substances, and the like, it will not be severed at all except rarely. If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not available, he should immediately place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
And he should tighten it very hard until the blood is constricted and flows away from above the wound and onto the artery, and the finger should not be removed from it, constantly using very cold water until the blood coagulates and thickens and stops.  In the meantime, consider what is needed in the way of cauterization or medication.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter, we mentioned every disease for which cauterization with fire and caustic medication is suitable, its causes, reasons, instruments, and the shapes of cauteries, making it chapters from head to foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, so that the student may easily obtain what he desires.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more dangers than the first chapter on cauterization.
And for that reason, warning should be more emphatic, because in the work of this chapter, there often occurs the loss of blood, upon which life depends, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor, or puncturing an abscess, or treating a wound, or removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and similar things, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them, death occurs.
And I advise you against engaging in what is doubtful for you, for various kinds of people may come to you in this craft with various kinds of illnesses; some of them may be weary of their disease and death may seem light to them.
