Because of the severity of their illness and the length of their affliction, and from the illness, there is that which indicates death; and some of them give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and their disease is deadly, so you should not at all cooperate with anyone who comes to you of this description.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with any of that except after certain knowledge is established with you of what leads to a favorable outcome; and in all the treatment of your patients, use the precedence of knowledge and warning of what leads to safety.
For in that, you have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance, and may He not deprive you of correctness and success; indeed that is in His hand, there is no god but He.
And I have arranged the chapters of this section in the same manner as what preceded in the chapter on cautery, from head to foot, so that what you want from it may be easy for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the water which collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often occurs to infants at birth if the midwife presses the infant's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone except infants, and all those I have seen of them have quickly approached death; therefore I have seen [it is best] to leave treatment. And I have seen an infant whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the infant could not sit up because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and the bone, or it collects under the bone on the dura mater.  The procedure in this is, if the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, then it is necessary to make one incision across the middle of the head, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.  This is an image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone—and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water subsides if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from you—then it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with cloths and bandages. Then you bathe it from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day. Then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments. Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with all dry food, low in moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the collection of water appear, because it may be more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appear to you, and you incise it as you are able, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the mastoids.
When someone is afflicted with severe colds in the eyes or chest, and this has persisted and medication has not been effective, then the most effective treatment is to sever these two arteries.
Then, if you wish to sever them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tie the patient's neck with a part of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their locations, the two low-lying places behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then sever them with a lancet, cutting to the bone.  This should be done across the head, or if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two fingers breadth.
If the artery is severed, blood will spurt forth in a pulsating manner, leaping forward in a continuous flow. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three fingers' breadth from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
Let six ounces of blood be released on average, though sometimes more or less may be released, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of his arteries. Then examine the wound; if any peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it away.
So that a hot swelling does not develop. Then bind the wound with a linen thread and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Incision of the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic hemicrania or acute attacks due to sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then in such diseases we have tried the incision or cauterization of the arteries of the temples as we described.
The procedure for their incision is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery visible in the temple should be sought, for it will be apparent to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or, in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with a part of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and evidently. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you insert a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides; if the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the hook. Then cut off a part of it to the extent that its ends separate and contract and do not cause bleeding; for if it is not severed and cut off, the blood will not flow at all.  Then bleed from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread, and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest decay reach it quickly before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, it will act as the same thing or better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization helps to eliminate humors and is therefore more beneficial. After removing the arteries, the place should be packed with old cotton and tightly bandaged. After healing, it should be treated with dry medicines that produce flesh and with dressings, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, quickly cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the place with vitriol and pressing your hand until the blood stops. If you do not have any of that, place your finger until the blood clots.
And wash the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and bind it as necessary.  What is easier than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two places with ink.
And apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that the ends are separated, for it will not heal at all with this cauterization. And this is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scissors, but much less sharp than a knife, because if they are as sharp as a knife, they cool more quickly and the flesh will not be severed quickly. If they are somewhat thick, the heat of the fire will remain in them.
And the flesh is cut quickly. This method is better than any other method, and easier.  The distance between the blades should be the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the outside of the skull and nothing from medical treatment with drugs has benefited, and you see the patient's face is reddened. And he feels a crawling on his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their lashes are eroded and their eyelids are clung together from the intensity of the tears.  The ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the scalpel; it is that you order the patient to have the hair shaved from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions on the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision is about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third from the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the joining of the jaws.  The distance between each incision should be about three fingers’ breadth. And have with you prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe away the blood.
Then you insert the double-pointed, sharp scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it you flay all the skin between the incisions, together with the periosteum on the bone. Then you also do this from the middle incision to the third incision, then you remove this scalpel. And you also insert into the first incision another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth, not sharp, on the other side. And this is its image:
And you place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and you push it until it reaches the middle incision, and you cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then you do that in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood flows, you squeeze the places of the clotted blood, then you place in each incision a wick of cotton, and you place over it a compress moistened with syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur, and on the third day you untie the bandage and use much irrigation with lukewarm water, then you treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and whatever else you treat wounds with, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences many, sharp, severe, persistent discharges, and you see his eyes weak and small, and his sight weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated, and the eyelashes falling from them. And there is a sharp, painful ache in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment in this is this treatment: that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then you make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is a sharp, painful ache in the depth of the head and continuous sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins.  The best treatment in this is this treatment: that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then you make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher across, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and let the incision be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples which move when chewing, until the bone is uncovered and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the lips of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then bind from above with compresses, and soak the compresses with syrup and oil, lest a hot swelling occur. And when you unbind them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, then you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins in it.
Then treat it with the drying treatment which causes the growth of flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpeas and kernels and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  When a stone or a type of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  Then if you see the stone, instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a sternutatory. And block the nostrils when sneezing comes, after you place a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extend the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
Seal around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a path for the air other than the tube. Then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out by this. If it does not come out by what we described, then take a little of the gum of Nabataea or of the prepared gum which is used by birds, and place it on the end of the probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it into the hole of the ear gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out by all that we described, then proceed to incision before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the temple and take out blood according to his strength. Then you sit the patient before you and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its lobe, in its lower part.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then stitch up the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear.  You do this only if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or a soft sponge or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove it.  As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with a nasal douche, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear containing the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly in the fire, and let the patient insert one into the opening of his ear. He should then hop on one foot on that side and strike the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this, stone by stone, until all the water comes out. The water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and applying the other end to a fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as previously described for the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small in size like a flea or similar; treat it as described in the classification, but…
if its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try to remove it with lint and hooks. This is easier than anything else that lodges in the ear.  As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you have treated it as mentioned in the classification in the chapter on instillations and your treatment has not worked…
you should examine the ear in the sun. If you see any worms, remove them with lint or fine hooks. If you see nothing, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly. Do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not respond to this, seal around the tube with wax as I described for the stone.
If they do not come out by the methods mentioned, use the instillations I have described, which have been tried by the ancients in killing worms, and which you will find in the chapter on instillations. Pouring oils and medicines into the ear should be done with this instrument, and this is its shape:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitting copper; or if you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear; then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the oil is forced into the inner ear and the patient feels it.
The patient should feel it entering.  What you pour into the ear should be warmed slightly over a fire. Be careful that what you drop in is neither too cold nor too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of the obstruction affecting the ear.
Some children are born with their ear canals not perforated.
Also, some people may experience an obstruction in the ear canal from a wound or from  growing flesh. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal and not visible to the eye.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, difficult to see. That which is visible should have the patient's ear placed in the sun. And look into it; if you see the obstruction, open it with a delicate scalpel, which is of this shape:
Its tip should be slightly broad and limited, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, slender probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient feels a heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then insert into the ear a tent corresponding to the size of the opening, having smeared it with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure that the wound has healed. Or take a tent, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol upon it.
And use it. If you see that the ear has swollen with a hot swelling, then it is necessary that you remove the tent and replace it with another tent smeared with a poultice made with rose oil, until the hot swelling subsides. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on cauterizations.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  It is necessary to grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered vitriol to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is best after cutting.  They often return if any of their roots remain; if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and they do not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness often appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, being a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment is to look: if the cold swelling is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every side, then the matter is easy; make a transverse incision upon it.
Then peel it off gently from every side until it is removed, then hang it with a hook and cut it off. If you are unable to cut it off except after penetrating the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, bring it together with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm from it, for ointment will heal and join it. If the cold swelling is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and hang the swelling with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it away from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm. Then wash the eye after cutting the swelling with salt water, and treat the area with something that will heal until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the stye which appears in the eyelid.
A stye is a fleshy growth which forms in the layers of the upper eyelid.  This most often affects children, weighing down their eyes and causing them to suffer from colds at night; they are unable to look at the sunlight. Because the tears rush to them.
Therefore you see them always leaning on their faces, and on them they sleep, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, swollen, and the stye is clearly visible. When you press the place with your fingers you feel the stye between them.  The method of treatment is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth and prepare from it a wick, and you make from that wick a circle of a size to encompass the stye from every side, then you place it on it and press with your fingers from every side to collect the stye in the middle of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, a transverse incision, and the incision should not be larger than the incision which is made in bloodletting; as for the depth, you should cut all the skin until you reach the stye.
Then, in most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat, so you pull it out with a cloth that you have wrapped between your fingers, while you turn your hand right and left until it heals. And beware of increasing the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and harm it.
If the stye does not appear to you at the first incision, then you should increase the incision a little gently until the stye appears, then pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the place and bind it with a bandage. And some people crush salt.
And they put it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then you treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On types of eyelid inflammation.
If there grow on the eyelid extra eyelashes, not in the natural course, under the natural eyelashes and eyelids, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, whiteness and thickening, until this becomes a cause of blindness in the eye.
Eyelid inflammation is of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the inflammation may be by cutting and suturing, or by a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it turns, otherwise, insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with thread from above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upward with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise in the inner eyelid, without the extra hair, with a sharp scalpel from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus; then remove the thread and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton or cloth, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, because people differ in this; some need to have a suitable amount cut from the eyelid according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less cutting, all that according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines which you marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and let each incision be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then flay it all, then bring together the edges with sutures using a fine woolen needle and thread, wipe away the blood, and stick what remains of the threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive material.
If you wish to do that, otherwise it doesn't matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid will rise when the wound is closed and joined; and suturing is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is troublesome for the patient; it is a good procedure and there is no danger in it. Another method of blepharoplasty is to mark on the eyelid a shape like that of a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, evenly, in this manner:
If you cannot hold the hooks and they do not remain steady for you, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger corner and a third thread near the smaller corner; and gather the threads between your fingers evenly, then raise them with an even lift.
Raise all of the marked skin as we described, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation occurs when you cut or incise, soothe that inflammation with Qirūṭī and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also turn in its margins; in this case, you should use what we mentioned: cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire, and with a caustic medicine. Cauterization with fire or with a caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and you should perform cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you turn back the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then you make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you cut into their ends on both sides where the threads will be attached, then gently bring them together on what remains of the eyelid, and firmly tie the two reeds on both sides, and leave it for several days; then the constricted skin dies, turns black and decays.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it off with scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it unites, the eyelid is raised and the margins no longer turn in. This is an image of the two reeds; make them in this shape and this exact size in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising ingrown eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle and thread it with a smooth, fine silk thread, then bring the two ends together and tie them in a very gentle knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another fine thread to the needle, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then the patient places his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clear, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread into the root of the ingrown hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards on the needle and insert into the needle that hair, whether it is one, two, or three, no more.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the needle and the hair does not come out with it, pull the needle downwards by the thread which you had attached to it, until the needle comes out from the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them both; do this until it rises to the upper opening and you see it with your own eye. Then remove the needle and thread and take them out, and bind the eye and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long hair from the natural hair so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a time, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye which occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
The condition to which this stye is exposed is called arnabiyya, and this stye is either natural or accidental.  The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or cut or cauterization and the like.
And the method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips and place between them a wick of linen and bind it until it heals, and it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was, but use in it things which relax, like fenugreek and lint with water in which mallow and flaxseed have been boiled, and diachylon ointment melted with something of the fats and smeared on the wick and treated with it.
And the key to its treatment is to try by every means that it does not adhere in the way it was originally, and this treatment which we have mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not that the appearance of the place returns to what it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of the stye which forms in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a stye, and it is natural and it is accidental. The accidental one is from a wound or incision or cautery and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with a doubled thread and you pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at the two ends of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise you take a probe and place it on the place of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the two incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet and a corner is formed from them. Until when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
And it is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is lower than what is next to the eyelid, then you gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of the relaxing and softening medicines until it heals.
And if the fistula appeared from an incision or suture or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the labia as well, as mentioned before, then separate the lips with a probe as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of the fistula, whether it is from above or below, you perform the procedure according to what is available to you of the appearance of the fistula, for it may vary greatly in form, and the skilled craftsman devises a solution in whatever way is possible for him.
Until the shape returns to its natural state or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates what is suitable for it in terms of procedure and instrument in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from easy movement and hindering its natural functions; therefore, you should insert the point of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel, not sharp like cutting scalpels, but rather a little blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness.  And your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed. Then pour into the eye some salt water to wash it, or instill into it some solution of vitriol dissolved in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread.
And place over the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the astringent medications until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the pterygium and the fleshy excrescence of the conjunctiva.
The pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, which resembles a thin, firm membrane; or non-nervous, which resembles a thick white moisture; if touched by an instrument or attempted to be removed with a hook, it is torn.
And a hook is not fixed in it. Both kinds begin from the greater canthus until they gradually spread until they cover the pupil and obstruct the light and the movement of the eye. The method of operating is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards; then you take a needle and thread it with a horsehair or cow hair and strong thread, and you bend the point of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
Then you pass the needle through and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards, and you dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you were spreading it with the hair to its end; then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
So that continuous lacrimation may not result from its excision. The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth end is dissected with a scalpel, whose image is this:
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest a wound occur in it and the pupil be injured. Then after the excision, you put some powdered salt or vitriol into the eye and leave it until the next day, then you treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you cannot insert a needle into it nor secure a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medications and it heals as described in the classification. If you wish to excise it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, in this image: And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and disintegrating and the dissection has affected it, then instill in the eye immediately some vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until all of it is gone, unless a hot inflammation interferes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the procedure of dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the caruncle…
If the excrescence harms the eye excessively, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it, but do not be excessive in cutting, lest it cause epiphora. Then instill red or vitriol in the caruncle until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On excising polypi and what grows of excessive flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red fleshy growth may sprout, accumulating until it covers the pupil, or comes near to it, or overflows onto the eyelids, and perhaps the eyelids turn outward; it resembles a pomegranate flower.
The procedure is that the patient places his head in your lap, then opens his eyes, and you grasp all that red flesh with hooks appropriate for that.
Or you grasp it with forceps or a small knife, then you cut the flesh, piece by piece, until you have removed it all by cutting, and you protect the eye so that it is not harmed during the procedure, and your cutting of it is either with the scalpel we described in cutting the membrane or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as its image comes in the chapter following this chapter.
When your cutting is completed and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with finely ground salt, or instill into it some red vitriol or similar caustic medicine, and apply to the outside of the eye a cotton compress with egg white to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overwhelms you and you fear hot inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with what calms the inflammation, then return to it with the procedure until it heals.  Do the same with excessive flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by circumstance with instruments, and do not put your hand to any of these procedures until you see that the member can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
On removing growths from the eye.
Growths are red vessels that grow on the eye, preventing its function and weakening the eye over time.  You should first look; if the eye in which the growths are found is strong and has no other disease besides the growths,
then the removal is performed by having the patient place his head in your lap, then you will hang those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill; and let the hooks be delicately curved in this manner:
Or let there be two double hooks in one body, in this manner:
Then, with delicate scissors, gently remove those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that those vessels have gone from the eye and the bleeding has stopped. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done midday, facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work, lest you cut anything other than those vessels.  Upon completion, instil green or red eye drops to cauterize any remaining growths. If you cannot remove them all at that time, then bandage the eye with something that soothes the eye's pain.
And leave it for some days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is safe, then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it is healed. This is the image of the scissors.
And leave it [and let the patient's head be in the lap of the student, and another wipes away the tears and examines the front and back of the eye, and you]. The principle of the procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular dilators held by a student.
Sharp, and let the patient's head be in the lap of the student, and another wipes away the tears and examines the front and back of the eye, and you have the option of beginning to remove [vessels] from the top or bottom of the eye, and it is preferable to remove two vessels in the upper corner, one at the end of the eyelid and the other at its beginning.
And grasp them between the middle and index fingers, then hook from below opposite them, then grasp them between the little and ring fingers, and lift your hand gently until you see all the vessels raised, then cut them with the smaller scissors if you wish, or with a delicate scalpel if you wish.
And you insert the thread onto a needle and you raise all the vessels until they rise above the pledget, and do not cut the [vessels] while they are still wet, for the tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the eyelid from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can raise it from the cornea by means of the thread, that is best, and it may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its place be cauterized for several days to alleviate the pain and prevent hot swelling, then resume work on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the nose to its place.
Physicians call a polypus a nāsūr. If you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as described above, and it does not heal, then there is no remedy but to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to evacuate all the moisture or pus within.
until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then remove it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp-head, made of Indian iron, is so called. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp.  You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then turn it between your fingers, while slightly pressing your hand, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Do this repeatedly, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medicaments. If the wound closes and flesh grows over it, and the discharge ceases, and forty days pass without recurrence, suppuration, or other incident,
know that it is healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy but to return the polypus to the nasal cavity in this manner:  You again expose the bone with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicament. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the point, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid bone and slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it if he holds his hand to his nose.  Then dress the wound with the drying, astringent medicaments as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the area becomes firm, then the material which was discharging externally will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no damage or impairment occurs in the vision, then you should ease the patient, and then you should bleed him in the cephalic vein. Then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without any specific requirement, and apply gentle suction.
Then apply a poultice made of acacia, aloe, frankincense, and gum tragacanth to the eye. Then bind the eye from above to the poultice with many pledgets. The method of making pledgets is to take many soft pieces of linen, and fold each piece into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one over the other, as much as the eye requires, and then bind it tightly with a firm cloth. Repeat the cupping with suction without any specific requirement, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, then it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, repeat the poultice, pledgets, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On cutting the uvula.
If a hernia appears in the uvular layer, then it swells and protrudes outside the eyelids like a grape, and the appearance of the person is disfigured by it, and you wish to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the root of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the uvula, and pass it through, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it tightly, then you remove the needle and place moistened wool on the eye with egg white and leave it until the threads fall out with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  And you may pull the uvula from each side with a circle of cloth until the eye swells more tightly, then you cut it with a thin scalpel that reaches the depth of the eye, so that the white moisture flows out, and the eye descends and withers in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of the uvula.
This ailment which is called *kumana* is a moisture that gathers in the eye, resembling descending water, but it is not. The procedure is that you seat the patient upright on a chair, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the moisture go to the bottom with your eye, then it becomes fixed and does not move, and the light departs and the patient sees things as he used to see them. And if it does not descend to the bottom, we know that it is water. If the descent of the moisture is not achieved by what we have mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision with it above the cornea at the point where the conjunctiva joins the cornea in the limbus until the material comes out.
When it has come out, instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for the cupping, with a detailed and explained statement; take that from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and bind it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which is the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye on the side of the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while you turn it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent to which the cupping instrument goes into depth is the extent of the distance that is from the pupil to the end of the black, which is the cornea, for you see the copper in the very gaze of the observer, by the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place in which is the water.
Then you press it downwards time after time; if the water descends immediately, then the patient sees what his sight has been opened to immediately, and the cupping instrument is in his eye, then it rests a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
Then if it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping instrument gently while you twist it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water a little of pure Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then apply to the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and you bandage it with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place on it crushed cumin with egg white. If the cupping instrument does not go into the eye because of its hardness—for some people have a very hard eye— Then you should take the scalpel which is called the *barīd*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not prolong the perforation; it is only that you make a gentle opening for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we have mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm bed in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food whatever softens his constitution, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
And you show him things, then you return the bandage until the seventh day.  And it is not proper to do this at the time of treatment or after cupping from your hour, but rather you should avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense sight.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh day and treat that with what calms the swelling until, when it subsides, then you release his sight and place a veil over his face, training his sight from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He leaves the house gradually and manages his affairs. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot dispense with observation many times; then he proceeds to work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that they make in Iraq a perforated cupping instrument with which they draw out water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that nor read it in any book of the ancients.  And it is possible that this is recent.  And this is an image of types of cupping instruments for one ignorant of them to stand upon.
It is made of copper especially, and its end is of this very thinness, triangular-tipped and sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the growth of flesh in the nose.
Various fleshy growths may grow in the nose, some resembling a centipede, some being cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some being soft flesh not dark in color.
As for those fleshy growths which are soft, not malignant nor cancerous, the patient should be seated before you facing the sun, his nostril opened; then you insert a snare into the flesh and draw it outwards, and cut off what you have grasped with a fine, sharp scalpel on one side.
Until you know that all of the flesh has gone; if any remains which you cannot cut, remove it gently with one of the fine instruments, so that nothing remains. If the bleeding is excessive, or hot inflammation occurs,
counteract it appropriately. If it is one of the malignant tumors, act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone; then place vinegar and water, or a medicated drink, in the nose. If the nose opens and the moisture passes down to the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that inside there is a fleshy growth on the top of the porous bones, which the instrument did not reach in cutting. Then you should take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, leaving the space of a finger or less between each knot.  The patient should skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or any suitable instrument, after shaping it like a button, and draw it through until it reaches the pharynx.
and it will come out at his throat. Children often do this in school, and it is easy for anyone who wants to. Then bring together the two ends of the thread, the one which came out of the mouth and the other which remains in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw away the flesh, doing so until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread, and after wiping away the blood, place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment in the nose. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh. Then, finally, place a lead tube in the nose for some days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of the speculum with which ointments and medications are instilled into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small, open, anointed candlestick, and its channel is likewise.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the surface of the speculum smooth.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, by which it is held when the ointment or any other liquids or flowing substances are heated within it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Frequently a wart grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and to excise it completely, then to apply to the place either cautery or a caustic medicine which takes the place of cautery. If it escapes cutting until it becomes large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not apply iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor; and I have often seen that cutting this tumor resulted in great harm to the patient. But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be completely cut, then purge the patient and cut it off without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with what dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment is rarely effective except in some people.
So if something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with blood; bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and then treat it until it heals.  If the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part is clean, then you should scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle on them frankincense and mastic, powdered.  Then place on top of the sutures a plaster of palm-ointment or another of the vulnerary ointments and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it.
And change the dressing and leave it until the threads fall out by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I showed you there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble small berries, others are smaller or larger. You should evert the lip and incise each nodule, and hang it with a hook.
And cut it from every side, then pack the site after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with an astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess flesh in the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hook it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base, and allow the serum or blood to flow, then apply powdered alum to the site.
Or one of the astringent drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatment—for it often returns—cut it again and cauterize it; it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with an iron instrument.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums; they may turn black, yellow, or green, so that the gums become diseased and the teeth unsightly because of it.
You should seat the patient before you with his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or teeth on which you see the crusts and sandy-like substance, until nothing remains; and do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness and whatever else you find, until they are clean.
If this removes what is there from the first cleaning, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then a second and a third time, until you achieve what you want. Know that a molar requires different types of scrapers, many shapes, according to what is suitable for your work.
Because the instrument with which you clean the molar from the inside is not the same as that with which you clean it from the outside, nor that with which you clean between the molars, which is of yet another form.  These are several forms of scrapers which you should have, all prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the molar for its pain by every means, and delay its extraction; for there is no replacement for it once it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when its extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is determined on its extraction, then you should be certain that the molar is the source of the pain. For often the patient is deceived by the pain, and thinks that it is in the healthy tooth, and extracts it; then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you are certain which tooth is the source of the pain, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel, so as to loosen the gum from every side; then move it gently with your fingers or with delicate forceps little by little, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having steadied it so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight out, lest you break it.  If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it from every side gently, and continue moving it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, then you should fill that hole with a cloth and pack it firmly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull it with the forceps; and you should thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
And take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient a greater affliction than his original pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do in their haste and recklessness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or that it is extracted with part of the jawbone, as we have often witnessed; then let him rinse his mouth after extraction with a drink or with vinegar and salt; and if bleeding occurs from the place—and this often happens—then crush some vitriol and pack the place with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the vitriol does not help you.  The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should be long-pointed, short-handled, and thick, lest they bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick-handled, so that when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend; short-pointed, and let them be of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, the points sharpened, and at the tips, teeth that interlock, so that they grip firmly and tightly.
And the points may be made in the shape of a file, and they will also be strong in their grip.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the extraction of the roots of molars and the removal of fractured jaw bones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken, you should apply a pledget of cotton soaked in butter to the area for a day or two, until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose points resemble the beak of the bird called the balarjah.  And this is an image of the hooks:
They should be made like a file from the inside, or like a scalpel. If you are unable to remove it with these hooks, you should excavate the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument resembling a small lever,  which is depicted thus:
short at the end, somewhat thick, and not pointed, lest it break. If the root is removed by this means, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted thus: the first is triangular at the end, somewhat thick:
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, which is also depicted thus:
And other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth.  We may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted thus:
Triangular at the bent end, somewhat thick, lest it break; and it should not be pointed. And know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, and so too are all other instruments; they are almost innumerable, and the skillful and clever craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the variety of their types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken, or becomes putrid, then search for it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks which were mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a curette, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to control the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed, and we dress the place with suitable medicines. If the bone has slight decay, scrape away its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the extraction of molars growing upon others.
If molars grow other than in their natural place, this is unsightly, especially if it happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another molar,
and its extraction or repositioning is impossible, then extract it. And if it is attached to another molar, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron.  Cut it in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, lest you shake other teeth. But if it is prominent and its removal is possible by filing, file it with an Indian file, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, very finely engraved, like the file used to make needles. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, lest you shake the tooth and it falls out. Then smooth it and remove it with some forceps.
And if a tooth is broken and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the fracture is removed, it becomes smooth, and it does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not been effective.
The solution is to tie them with a gold or silver thread, gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever; and the thread should be of moderate thinness and thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach the sound tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then you weave back to the side where you began, and gently and skillfully tighten the thread so that it does not move at all; you should tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth lest it loosen.  Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them together, and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Then leave it tied as long as it remains; if it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread, and he will enjoy it thus for his whole life.  This is an image of the teeth and the method of binding in two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see:
And a single tooth or two may be returned to their place after falling out and bound as we have described, and remain.  This is done by a skilled dentist. He may also carve a piece of ox bone, shape it like a tooth, and place it in the space from which the tooth has fallen. And it is tightened as we have said, and remains, and he enjoys that.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound which has healed.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its constraint. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and that is from a healed wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is undone.
And beware that the incision is not deep into the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then let him rinse the mouth after the cutting with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of lint which the patient holds each night.
Lest it unite again. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the place. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter thirty-five.
On removing the frog which develops under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small frog sometimes occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps it grows until it fills the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth towards the sun and examine the swelling.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard and insensitive to the patient, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer; but if it is inclined to whiteness and moist, insert a hook into it and incise it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every side.
If the bleeding is excessive during your operation, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you remove it completely. Then have him gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors;  glands may appear inside the throat similar to those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what was mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with an instrument. And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with an instrument either, for fear of hemorrhage; rather, leave it until it matures, then either cauterize it or it will burst of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or lessened somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth. Take an assistant with you, who will press his tongue downwards with an instrument of this shape:
Make it of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue with it and the tumor becomes visible to you, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outwards as much as possible without pulling anything of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape, resembling scissors except that its two ends are curved, and the edge of each is very sharp. Make it from Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
Sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same way. After cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops. Then treat him until he recovers. Other tumors besides the tonsils may grow in the throat; cut them as I described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman who had a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, which almost blocked her throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she remained for a day or two.  Two branches extended from the tumor, reaching her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one of them and pulled; a suitable piece came away.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, depressed her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it; only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat.  We repeatedly cut away parts of the tumor over a long period; the tumor replaced what was cut until this treatment continued for a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat.  It stopped growing, then she left the area and I did not know what God did with her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
In cutting the uvular tumor.
That which is called a grape, if it descends to the uvula and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a pillar; and if it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a grape.  If it is treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and the treatment is ineffective, …
And I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin.  Then it should be cut, and what was gathered together and round, and did not have length, and was bloody or the color of pus, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting.
For there is danger to the patient.  Therefore, if you see it in the manner that I mentioned, of its whiteness and length, then the patient should sit facing the sun, and you should compress his tongue with the instrument which I previously described, then pierce the grape with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils.  It is necessary that you cut only that which exceeds the natural state, without excess; for if you cut more than that, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method of cauterizing it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and that is that you place the head of the patient in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument which we mentioned, then take of the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the cavity of this instrument with it.  And this is its image:.
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a cavity like the cavity of a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest anything descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you press it against the uvula for about half an hour, until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe and moisten the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, then leave it; it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine from around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat from the outside with poultices and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take fumitory, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and costus, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil it, and the pot is plastered with wise clay, and there is a hole in the middle of the lid, upon which is fitted a hollow instrument made in this manner, made of silver or copper.
And insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is well-fumigated. Then repeat it several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first occurrence of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling. Rather, it should be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and attach an eggshell to its end lest the patient's mouth be burned.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is among the good treatments along with its safety.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone or fishbone or something else lodges in the throat.  It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye, after you have pressed down the tongue with the instrument toward the sun.
So that you may discern what is in the throat. And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you cause the patient to vomit before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged thing will come out with the vomit. Or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or a head of lettuce.
Or let him swallow a mouthful of dry bread. Or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, then let him swallow it.  When it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly. Do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a probe, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it into his throat gently, raising his head upwards, and taking care not to touch his throat, lest he cause coughing and push the bone or thorn further down. Or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, for he knows the position of the lodged thing.  He pushes it downwards or pulls it upwards with the instrument, doing all this as much as he is able, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you have treated the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medical treatment and it has not been effective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight, after you have pressed down his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or with a fine, strong forceps.  If you cannot do this, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a hot iron into the tube.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from water all that day, then take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it without swallowing a drop, moving the water around from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it feels the water. If it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with galbanum using the instrument described for fumigating the tonsils; do this several times, for then it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is as follows: take a pot containing hot coals, the pot being covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole; you then fit the end of the instrument into this hole, and throw in the fumigant. The patient places his mouth at the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape, until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out. If it does not fall out, the fumigation should be repeated several times. The patient should endure thirst, eat salt and garlic, and drink no water; the leech must come out with this treatment.  This is an image of an instrument for drawing out a leech from the throat if it is visible.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its ends resemble a bird's beak, having the roughness of a file; if it grips something, it does not let go.
Another instrument is used to compress the tongue. It is a wide iron ring, large enough to fit into the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth upright on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he is completely unable to remove it until it is taken away.
The description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this way.
Chapter Forty.
It will then speak of cauterizing and incising tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be described one by one in this book. They differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not the same as a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete; others should be cauterized while still raw and not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it causes what is around it to fester, it may destroy the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus causing crippling of that member, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that vital organ; or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it while raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a tumor near the rectum while it is raw and not fully matured, lest it fester and penetrate into the rectum, thus becoming a fistula.
And it will become something that will not heal. You should know the time to cauterize tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the tumor head softens, and all other signs subside.
And you should apply the cautery to the lowest possible part of the tumor, if that is possible, so that the discharge may flow down more easily, or to the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The cautery should be lengthwise, if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries; in short, in all flat places that do not bend.  But those that bend, the cautery should be applied according to that location. And if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, and it will be very harmful and dirty; and perhaps its lips and base will harden. Some tumors may be cauterized across the body, if necessary, or according to the needs of the affected part.
