You should use a single cautery for small tumors, and a wide cautery or many incisions for large tumors, according to the size of the tumor. Some tumors may need to have the skin scraped and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached a point where it is dead, as happens in many abscesses and fistulas.  Some are incised with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
And some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions.  And if it does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, then it should only be cauterized simply. And if the tumor is large, and has collected for a long time, and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until another day, then remove some more of the pus; do this repeatedly gradually until you remove it all.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, so the patient may die without you noticing; therefore beware of this matter with great caution.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and examine it. If the wound of the tumor is small, or the incision is single and simple, then use a lint of linen or old cotton.  And if the tumor is large and the incisions from cauterization are numerous,
then you should insert a wick into each incision until some of them reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or scraped away, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture, and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the management, treatment, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the section. If you are in winter and the location of the tumor is very nervous, then you should moisten the pledgets with warm syrup and oil.
And place them on the site. And if you are in summer and the locations are fleshy, then you should apply pledgets soaked in water and oil or in syrup and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should unwrap the tumor and wipe it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor, categorized and summarized.
Chapter forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear in the scalp.
Small tumors appear in the scalp; they are a type of boil contained within membranes, their conditions resembling a chicken’s crop.  Their types are numerous: some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
Some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on; and some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing them all, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the *muddass*, so that you know what they contain. If what they contain is moisture, then incise them lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line B to line C. When the moisture has been emptied, then peel away the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, leaving nothing of it whatsoever, for it often returns if anything of it remains.
Then dip cotton in  *marham sirri* if it is present; otherwise, in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, for it will eat away what remains of the sac. Then apply the cotton with *marham sirri* again and a third time if you need to.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals.  If the swelling contains fatty matter, then incise it in this manner:
A firm incision, and place hooks in the lips of the wound and peel it from every side, and exert yourself in removing the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, then do what I described to you before, and similarly do in the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
And the treatment is the same as I mentioned, and the incision on the hardened swelling is easier because it is low in blood and moisture.  And some of these swellings which are in the head in some people have no moisture with them at all; and that is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone; it could not be broken, and if someone were to throw it, it would cut him. And it was not like the other swellings occurring in the head, other than this, like the swellings which appear in the heads of children.
And at the bases of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision, and make their bottoms from their lower parts so that the flow of the matter to the lower part is easy, then treat them with what suits them from treatment.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These swellings frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and they may appear in the rest of the body.  And that which appears in the neck of them may be one, or it may be many, and some of them develop from others.
And every swelling of them is within a special membrane, as is the case in the boils and swellings of the head as we have described, and the types of these swellings are many; some of them are calcified, and some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
So what I saw of them that was good in condition to the touch, and its appearance was near to the color of the skin, moved to every direction, and was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it deep-seated. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body in this manner, from line J to line B.
And peel it from every side and stretch the lips of the skin with a hook or with two hooks or with many hooks if it needs that, as we said in the swellings of the head, and remove it little by little, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and let the scalpel not be very sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting or the patient be troubled, so you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders your work, then place in the wound powdered verdigris or some of the things that stop blood.
And bind the wound and leave it until the acuteness of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding will then stop. Then return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if there remain other small tumors, then cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the tumor, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled, tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm. Then stuff the wound with old cotton which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if you cut the entire tumor, then it is necessary to bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains at all.  If you see that a residue of the bone of the tumor remains,
then it is necessary that you aim at cutting to its root and base and use sutures and what we have mentioned. And as for tumors containing fluids, apply a simple poultice where their maturation appears, and make the poultice from what is below, as we said.
Then, after the poultice, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or the like to consume what remains of the corruption, until when you see that the wound has been cleansed, then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words; as for those with angina, they should avoid the incision of the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak; but those who have a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the windpipe, then it is necessary to use the incision of the throat to escape the harm that results from suffocation.
Then, if we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent that the incision is in the membrane, not in the cartilage; this place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are far from it. If the physician is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the membrane that we described.  He will know that the windpipe has been incised by the phlegm that comes out of it, along with what is excised, and by the interruption of the voice. The wound should be left open for a time, and when the time during which suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound should be joined together with the skin.
Its incision is solely in the membrane, apart from the cartilage. Then use medications that generate flesh until it heals. The author of this book said, "The explanation of all that we have mentioned is that if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these tumors which have been mentioned.
And if the patient is close to death and his breath is about to cease, they resort to incising the throat so that the patient breathes through the wound, breathes somewhat, and is saved from death. And they are ordered to leave the wound open until the course of the disease is complete, and its course is about three days or so.
Then they are ordered to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and ran it across her throat, cutting some of the windpipe. I was summoned to treat her, and I found her weakening as a slaughtered animal weakens.  I uncovered the wound.
I found the blood that came from the wound slight, so I was certain that she had not cut a vein or jugular vein.  Air was coming from the wound. So I acted quickly, incised the wound, and treated it until it healed.  Nothing happened to the maidservant except for hoarseness in her voice, and nothing more.
And after days she returned to her best condition. From this we say that there is no danger in incising the throat.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision on the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside and is called the throat’s elephantiasis.
This tumor, which is called the throat’s elephantiasis, is a large tumor the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, and the other resembling the tumor that results from the complication of the artery, and there is danger in incising it. Therefore, one should not at all expose it to the iron, except that which is small, if you probe and examine it with a probe and find it to resemble a fatty swelling and not be attached to anything from the veins, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with whatever contains it from the sac if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove it all completely, then treat the place with whatever treatment is appropriate.
Chapter forty-five.
Concerning the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and terrible pains until the boiling of the pus subsides. And the pus matures; then the fever and intensity subside. A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain, and it is contained in a serous sac, which is its special covering. It is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or it contains moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the probe, whose form is shown in the chapter following this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If fluid of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision, as I mentioned in other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and suspend it with hooks. Carefully peel the skin from every side, and preserve the sac, if you are able, so that you remove it intact with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part, however small or large, remains, the swelling will usually recur.
If it overcomes you and a small amount remains, cauterize the wound at its completion with some sharp, corrosive cautery.  Then stitch the wound and apply something on top that cools hot swellings, and treat it with all other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, then suture its lips and treat it with things that promote healing.
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound is cleaned and the bleeding stops. Then return to cutting away what remains of the swelling.

Chapter forty-seven.
Concerning the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the age of puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly. Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medicine, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast inclines downwards and becomes flaccid due to its size, as happens to women,
it is necessary to make two crescent-shaped incisions in its upper sides, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then flay the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we have mentioned of sutures and dressings and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you are unable to cut away what should be cut away because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the excision of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors which appear under the armpit are of the kind of hard swellings contained in a sac, and some contain fluids.  As for those which contain fluids, an incision should be made in a crescent shape in this manner.
From line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the kind of hard swellings, make an incision as we mentioned in the incision for swellings. If the wound persists and pus forms, use cautery as we previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
In the incision of the tumor which appears near the artery or vein, and is called aneurism.
If the artery is injured and the skin over it heals, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a swelling and tumor can also occur in a vein.
The signs by which one knows if the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong, deep-seated in the body, and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a murmur.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body, and incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places of the body where they are very large; therefore, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided.  As for those which are a swelling of the mouth of the artery, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in ligating the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the place between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will resolve, and you use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor with the skin as you can, then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand and pass it through with a doubled thread until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, as I described to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out.  Then cut away the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then place on it a compress which you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment with cauterization and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor which appears from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor appears in an artery and vein, so too does a tumor appear in a nerve if a blow occurs to it or it happens from excessive fatigue or the like.  This is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a soft tumor, its color resembling the rest of the body. It is, in most instances, without pain. If pressed firmly, the patient feels a kind of numbness. The tumor is not clustered deep in the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, neither forward nor backward. As for that which is in the joints, it should not be approached with iron, for it might cause gangrene. As for that which is in the head or forehead...
then incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its root. If it is large, hook it with a snare and peel it off, then remove it, and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
On the excision of warts which appear in the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies.  These are called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their root is thin and their head is thick; their lips may be transformed, and some are small while others become very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen; they were very much like mushrooms, white and thin-rooted, their lips transformed and cracked, with moisture constantly flowing from them. I cut them off; one weighed about eighteen ounces, and the other about six.
In excising them, observe whether the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin-rooted. If so, cut it with a wide scalpel, having cauteries ready in the fire, for often much blood gushes forth when they are cut.
If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize it immediately. If you see that the patient is cowardly and afraid of cutting with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this type, leaving it for two days, then increasing the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Continue tightening until it affects the wart, causing it to separate and fall off by itself without effort. If the wart is thick-rooted, there is some danger and uncertainty in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut off half or some of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat it until the wound heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark-colored, insensitive, and foul-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor; we shall describe cancer later.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
In the treatment of umbilical hernias.
Umbilical hernias result from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as happens in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from wind that is contained therein.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft, painless, and of varying appearance. If it is due to the protrusion of intestine, its appearance, as we have described, will be more varied. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; and sometimes there is gurgling. It increases greatly with the entry into a bath and with great exertion. If it is due to moisture, then it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then, along with these signs, the tumor appears dark. If it is due to newly grown flesh, then the tumor is firm, hard, and remains the same size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The treatment is as follows: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or to wind, then you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and deception in that, as I have explained in the chapter where I mentioned the tumors that arise from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to intestine or fat, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand upright and extended, then you should mark with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then you should instruct him to lie on his back before you. Then you should incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place you marked with ink. Then you should extend the middle of the tumor upwards with a large hook, and then tie the place of incision with a strong thread or silk sinew, tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then open the middle of the drawn-up tumor above the ligature, and insert your index finger and search for the intestine. If you find that the ligature has grasped it, loosen the slip-knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find fat, extend it with a hook.
And cut away its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, crisscrossing them, having passed them through. Then tighten the tumor in four places over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the drawn-up flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it if it becomes thin and decayed, then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then it is necessary that you constrict the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will close the place.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
In the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how the way to treat it with medicines and the warning against treating it with iron lest it ulcerates, and we have mentioned the cancer that develops in the womb and the warning against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that whenever the cancer is in a place that can be completely excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar easily removable organs, especially if it is small and beginning, but if it is old and large, then you should not approach it.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me who has achieved that, and the procedure, if it is possible as we said, is that you first purge the patient of black bile several times, then you bleed him if there is clear fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows for the procedure, then you apply to the cancer the hooks suitable for it, then you scrape it from every side along with the skin completely until nothing of its roots remains, and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, press the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if you encounter during the procedure profuse bleeding from a severed artery or vein, cauterize the vessel until the bleeding stops, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of the boil.
We have explained in the classification the types of dropsy, how the water accumulates, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medicines.  That which is treated with iron is only the ascitic type alone; the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with iron.
For that is fatal. If you have treated this type of ascitic boil with medicines and your treatment has not been effective, then look: if the patient is extremely weak or has another illness besides the boil, such as…
a cough or diarrhea, and the like, then beware of treating him with iron, for that is dangerous.  If you see that the patient is strong, has no illness other than the boil alone, and is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is to have the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him squeezing his abdomen with his hands and pushing the water downwards towards the pubis; then take a lancet like this:
limited on both sides, long, with a defined tip like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not go too far into the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then observe if the boil has originated from the area of the intestines.
It is necessary that you make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubis. If the boil has originated from a liver disease, let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; if it has originated from a spleen disease, let the incision be on the right side, three fingers' breadth away.
Nor should the incision be on the side on which the patient wants to lie down, lest the pus flow to that weak place. Then pierce the skin completely with the instrument, then insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be about the size of a fingernail or so; then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches the empty space, which is the place of the water, then take it out and insert into the opening the instrument which has this shape.
It is like a small tube made of a thin reed, made of silver, or copper, or smooth, polished lead; it has a small opening at its lower end, and three openings on its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And one may sharpen its end in the shape of a pen nib, in this manner:
At its upper end is a ring; for when the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately flow down the instrument, and you will evacuate a moderate amount of water at a time; for if you evacuate more than is necessary at one time, the patient may die from the dissolution of his vital spirit.
Or he may suffer a faint that approaches death; but evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you, from the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion; then remove the instrument and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which covers the opening in the peritoneum, which I have instructed you to suture in that manner; then you may reinsert the instrument on another day, if you see the patient able to bear it.
And you will also evacuate a small amount of water; do this for several days, and you are responsible and must guard against error, until only a little water remains; but if you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat him by burial in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and patience with thirst, and treat him with drying medicines until he recovers; and cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after evacuating the water, as I have described above.
Chapter fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages imperforate, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their anus imperforate; it is necessary to perforate it as soon as they are born.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, and bind it and keep it there for three or four days.  When he wants to urinate, remove it, and after urination, replace it. If you do not use lead, it is possible that the urine flowing to the place will not allow it to close.
As for him whose opening is narrow, he is treated with lead as we have said for many days until it widens.  And those whose opening is misplaced, that is, some are born with the opening at the end of the rectum, and are unable to urinate forward.
Until he lifts the urethra upwards by hand, and it is not born before that, since the semen cannot reach the uterus straight, and this is a very ugly condition.  The method of treatment is that the patient lies on his back.
Then stretch his rectum with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the tip of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, the size of a pen, or as if you were carving something so that its middle protrudes, similar to a penis, and the opening falls in the middle as it should.
And be careful during your work to avoid bleeding, for this often occurs; therefore, stem the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter fifty-six.
Concerning the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a hard fleshy growth, and there is benign and malignant.  The benign should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you remove it all, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with date-palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, then cauterization should be used after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the ulcer that is inside first, until it heals; then treat the outside.
Because if you treat them both at the same time, you do not prevent the foreskin from being perforated.  And it may also appear in the labia and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption; then you should remove everything that has blackened and threatens to corrupt or has corrupted.
Then apply honey with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and  cress; then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery of this shape:
For cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean bleeding and the wound if it becomes corrupted. If the glans is eroded and completely destroyed at some time, then you should insert a lead tube into the urethra so the patient can urinate through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the glans…
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur due to a wound or swelling. Then you should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is broken and the glans is freed from every side.
If it is difficult to distinguish them completely, then you should separate some of the glans and some of the foreskin.  Because the foreskin is thin, it may quickly perforate due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the glans with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what befalls them of ailments.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and its use is especially in boys, it is necessary that we describe the best procedure in it.
And the easiest path leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience. And that is that I found the majority of the surgeons and barbers using cleansing with the knife and with the scissors, and they use the forceps and ligature with thread and cutting with the fingernail.  I have tried all these methods.
I did not find better than cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, because cleansing with the knife often injures the skin, because the skin of the prepuce is two layers, so perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
Then one is forced to make another cut and renewed pain, and cleansing with forceps does not prevent the cutting of the end of the urethra, because it might enter its hole. As for cleansing with the fingernail, the skin might slip and your work is spoiled, or the boy's skin might be naturally short...
so often they are born like that, not needing cleansing. I have seen that. As for cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, experience has revealed its advantage to me, because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, because the blade from above is like the blade from below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut with one measure and in one time, so the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all. The way to work, first, is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving him until another day, then rejoice and please him in every way you can and that he accepts with his mind, then have him stand before you erect.
And let him not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so the boy's eye does not fall on them at all, nor on anything of the instruments. Then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it up until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then cleanse it from whatever dirt gathers in it, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it, then hold with your thumb and forefinger the lower ligature firmly, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt dry gourd ash, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, for that too is excellent.
Then quickly lift the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some burnt dry gourd ash, for it is the best I have tried, or finely ground barley, for that too is excellent. Then apply over the wound from above a cloth of a boiled white egg in rosewater, beaten with fresh, fragrant rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the other treatments until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
Let them be sharp, cutting, not crooked, with a nail-like point, and let the length of the blades be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake which occurs in circumcision, sometimes the whole or part of the inner skin is inverted during the cutting, so you should stretch it out at once with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you are unable to hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and then remove it gently.
And cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the head of the urethra, for if something of the head of the urethra is cut, it does no harm; treat it with what heals the wound from among the caustics which we described in the chapter on caustics.
And if more of the skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, this also does little harm; treat it with what we have mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Urine retained in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth, or something similar.  If you treat this with the various treatments described in the classification,
and the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then the patient should be placed kneeling, then a man should mount his back, and stretch his belly towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow with what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use the instrument called a catheter for its removal, whose image is this:
It is made of silver, and it is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's quill in thinness, about a span and a half long, having a gentle funnel at its head. The method of drawing off urine with it is that you take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And you insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the catheter and cut with scissors any excess of wool so that what enters the tube blocks it like a button. Then you lubricate the catheter with oil, or butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
And you clean his bladder and urethra with moist ointments, or oil and lukewarm water. Then you insert the catheter into the urethra gently until it reaches the root of the urethra, then you bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then you push the catheter inside it.
Until, when the urethra is completely bent, then you push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the rectum, you incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside it, then you push it until it reaches the bladder and the patient feels it has reached something empty.
And it is made in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then you pull the thread with the wool gently; then the urine follows the wool, then you take it out and the urine flows, and you return the catheter.
You continue to do this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as thin as pus, and you intend to instill liquids and medicines into it.  This is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as thin as a hair, entirely hollow except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the piston is, should be just large enough to fit it, without excess, so that when you draw something up with it, it is drawn up, and when you push it, it is pushed out, just like the syringe that throws oil in sea battles.
If you wish to introduce liquids into the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the liquid and draw the piston upwards; then the liquid will be drawn into the body of the syringe. Then insert its tip into the urethra, as described in the section on catheters, and then push the liquid with the piston.
Then that liquid will reach the bladder immediately, until the patient feels it.  This is the image of a fine clyster also used to inject the bladder:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature is placed. Then take a camel's bladder and put in it the liquid you wish to inject into the bladder, and tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
Warm the liquid slightly over a fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then squeeze the bladder containing the liquid firmly with your hand until the patient feels that the liquid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into those holes as you go around, then gather the circle as you gather a cloth, after having placed in it what you wish of liquids, ointments, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, squeezing with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medication, and described the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all of that, and mentioned the stones in which one uses the hand and incision.
And that is the stone generated specifically in the bladder, and that which becomes fixed in the urethra, and I am describing that with a clear and concise explanation.
So I say that the stone generated in the bladder is mostly found in boys, and among its signs is that urine exits the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and it often hangs down then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them, and it is easy to remove from boys until they reach fourteen years, and it is difficult in the elderly, and as for young men, it is moderate between that.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small one is the opposite.  If we proceed to treatment, then it is necessary first that the patient be given an enema to expel all the feces in his intestines, for it may prevent the finding of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place several times, then you seat him between your hands erect, and his hands under his thighs, so that the whole bladder inclines downwards, then examine him.
And palpate him from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, immediately begin to incise it. If it does not fall under your touch at all, then you should lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully grown boy, then insert it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you wish to incise.
And you order a servant to compress the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch with his right hand the labia upwards, and with his other hand he stretches the skin under the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the lancet whose form is this:
And incise between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left buttock. The incision should be on the stone itself, and your finger in the anus, and you press it outwards obliquely so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that the stone can emerge from it.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the anus will press the stone during the incision and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, and thus their removal is difficult.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and rounded, and their removal is easy.  But those with angles and edges, increase the incision a little; and if it does not come out like this, then you should try to grasp it, either with a firm forceps whose end is like a file.
to hold the stone so that it does not escape from it; or else insert a delicate instrument with a hooked end underneath it. And if you cannot manage it, widen the incision a little. And if a quantity of blood overcomes you, stanch it with alum.
If there are more than one, push the largest one first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards; and thus you do if there are more than two.  If it is very large, then it is foolish to make a large incision upon it, because it exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either he will die, or he will suffer a constant dribbling of urine because the place will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or try to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with myrrh, aloes, and cyperus, and bind it, and place upon it a cloth moistened with oil and wine, or with rose oil and cold water, to calm the hot inflammation; then let the patient lie on his back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the place with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm-tree ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If there occurs in the wound excessive hot inflammation, ulceration, and the like, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urination – this is known by the passage of blood with the urine –
then insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood; for if it remains, it leads to corruption and suppuration of the bladder.  Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type with its corresponding therapy until it heals.
In all the times of treatment, it is necessary to bind and bring together the thighs to secure the medicaments which are applied to the place. If the stone is small and has entered the passage of the penis and become fixed in it, and urination is prevented, then treat it with what I have described before it comes to incision.
Often I have dispensed with incision by this treatment, which I have tested; it is to take a probe of steel, which has this shape:
The end triangular, sharp, fixed in a stick; then take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, lest it return to the bladder; then gently introduce the steel probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone itself little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through it to the other side; then the urine will flow immediately. Then press your hand on what remains of the stone from outside the penis, for it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will recover.
But if this treatment is not feasible for you because of some impediment that prevents you, then tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then incise over the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other ligature above is so that when the thread is untied after removal of the stone, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upwards so that it will return when you are finished and cover the wound, as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones from women.
Stones rarely develop in women; but if a woman is afflicted with a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin, and the second is that you will not find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or one of his relatives, and the third is that you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in manual work, and the fourth is that the place of the incision for the stone in women is distant from the position of the stone, so it needs a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought, but they are rarely found. If you cannot find one, then seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or get a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge in this craft.
Then you should prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order her to do in examining for the stone first. That is, to see if the woman is a virgin; then she should insert her finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If she finds it and presses it under her finger...
then you should order her to incise it. If she is not a virgin but a widow, then order the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it well.
If she finds it, then she should pass it from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as she can until she reaches the origin of the thigh, then she should incise it opposite the middle of the vagina at the origin of the thigh, from whichever side she can and feels the stone in that area.
And her finger should not move from the stone, pressing under it. Let the incision be small at first, then insert the probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, then increase the incision as much as she knows is necessary for the stone to come out. And know that the types of stones are many.
Some are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and branched.  Know their types so you can determine what you need. If the blood overcomes you, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
until the stone comes out. And make sure you prepare with yourself the instruments that I mentioned in removing stones from men to help you in your work. If the bleeding overwhelms you and you know from the pulse of the blood that it is from a severed artery, then place the forceps on the area.
And tighten it firmly with bandages and leave it; do not revisit it and leave the stone; do not remove it, for perhaps the patient will die. Then treat the wound. And if the severity of the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On the incision on the watery membrane.
The watery membrane is merely an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, and it is called the scrotum.  It may be in a special membrane prepared by nature in one part of the testicle.
So that one might think it is another testicle.  It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned, but this occurs rarely. This membrane is generated from weakness affecting the testicles, so this material gathers there.  It may also occur from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture is of many colors: sometimes it is yellowish, sometimes bloody red, sometimes dark and dirty, sometimes watery white, which is most common.  The signs by which it is known are...
Where the water gathers, if it is in the white peritoneum which we mentioned, then the swelling is somewhat elongated and round, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
And if the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Because of this, a person imagines that it is another testicle. And if the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you want to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image we presented earlier; what comes out after the probe, you judge by what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with cautery, it is necessary that you order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see that his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, and you place many cloths under him. Then you sit on his left.
And you order a servant to sit on his right, extending his penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the groin. Then you take a broad scalpel and incise the scrotum lengthwise from the middle to near the pubis.
And make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in half, until you reach the white peritoneum which contains [the testicle].  Then peel it back, and be careful not to incise it. Your peeling should be from the side that adheres to the testicle more, and you should complete the peeling as much as you can.
Then you widely open the peritoneum filled with water and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the peritoneum upwards, but do not touch the containing scrotum skin. Cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure that the water will not return. If the testicle emerges from its skin during your work, then when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it, and then join the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is already damaged by another disease, then it is necessary to ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have described.
If the collected water is in both sides, know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, and if it is convenient for you to do it all at once, then do so.  Then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool that you have soaked in oil or rose ointment, and on the outside place other wool soaked in a drink and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded cloths on top—these are the pledgets. And tie them from above with a bandage which has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this manner. The ends used to tie it are made of cotton or wet wool and gathered tightly in the waistband of the trousers; one end is taken to the buttocks and to the back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins. All the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers. This hydrocele may also be treated by cautery instead of incision with an instrument.
This is done by taking a gentle, knife-like cautery and incising the skin of the scrotum with it while it is hot, as we have described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Then take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye. Then cauterize that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and peel off the containing layer with the cautery, cutting it as much as you can until you excise all of it, being careful not to touch the testicle with the fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and prevents more bleeding, and is better than the incision method.  If during the incision or cautery you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, then you should treat whatever arises with the appropriate treatment as described above.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it is confirmed by the signs we have previously mentioned.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high stool, and then cauterize the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel. Make the cauterization along the length of the body, and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out. Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization because the tunica albuginea obstructs the opening of the wound and prevents the water from coming out,
then you should insert a goose feather or the instrument used to extract the water from hydroceles, the image of which was shown previously, or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and collect after about six months. If it collects, cauterize it in the same way, and the patient should abstain from intercourse during those days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
On the incision on the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this membrane is very dangerous and often leads to death; therefore, we advise leaving it alone and avoiding it, although I will mention how to treat it and the various types of swelling.
I say that many swellings occur in the bodies which constitute the female genitalia; this occurs for many reasons, either from excessive heat descending to the genitalia, or from a blow. The swelling is the same colour as the body and contains no water.
The swelling may be soft, or it may be hardened, dark in colour and insensitive. It may result from the obstruction and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins.  If it is from the swelling of the arteries, this is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not disperse. As for that which results from the swelling of the veins, nothing of it disperses when pressed with the fingers. This type may be incised, along with the surrounding flesh.
You should incise the skin of the testicle, then draw the testicle upwards and bring it out from the white peritoneum. Free the spermatic cord from the vessels, ligate the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from all sides of the testis. If the testicle is adhered to those growing tissues.
Then you should remove and cut the testicle. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between the vessels, you should free and cut all that adhesion with a circular cut.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, you should cut it all away and remove the testicle as we said. When your work is complete, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-four.
On the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the testicles, making movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient. The treatment of this condition is similar to what has been mentioned before.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that you should have the patient sit on a high chair, then push the testicle upwards, then grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels which are near the penis.
And let an assistant hold it and stretch it tightly, then you incise with a broad, sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed, then dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of arteries in the temples, then insert a needle with doubled thread.
And tie it at the first point where the hernia appeared and tie it also at the end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along its length and remove the collected turbid, corrupted fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal for a while, so that the vessels that nourish one of the testicles fall off; there is no harm in that.  If the hernia affects all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one testicle with the vessels, lest the testicle lack nourishment. before cutting the vessels, so that they wither and are of no use.
Chapter sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen near the groins from the abdominal cavity, so the intestine descends from that hernia into one of the testicles.
And this hernia is either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, and the like.  Its sign, if it is from an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even to the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine.  Its sign, if it is from a fissure in the peritoneum, is that it initially causes great pain suddenly, and the swelling is uneven, appearing under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  And the omentum may exit with the intestine, and this hernia is called intestinal and omental. And it may be with wind, and the feces may flow into the intestine and be retained there, resulting in the death of the patient.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted.  Treatment of these types of illness with cautery is dangerous, so one should be wary of it. The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to return the intestine by hand into his abdomen if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upward and incise all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
And the incision should be such that the testicle can exit from it. Then you dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then insert your index finger into the area behind the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and with it you loosen the adhesions behind the testicle. Then, with the right hand, you draw it toward the inside of the scrotum's skin.  With this, you stretch the white peritoneum upward with the left hand and raise the testicle with the peritoneum toward the incision.
And you instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upward, and you completely loosen the adhesions from behind.  And you search with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum. If you find something, push it down into the abdomen.
Then you take a needle with thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin next to the incision. Then cut the ends of the thread's twist until there are four threads. Then you place some of them on top of each other firmly, and with them you tie the peritoneum—which we said is under the skin of the scrotum—tightly on two sides. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave of the peritoneum under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave of the peritoneum under the skin of the scrotum the amount of a finger bone. And you cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then you make an incision at the bottom of the scrotum's skin from which blood and pus will flow, as we described before. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature that we described.
And one may cauterize the white peritoneum which we mentioned after cutting it, cautiously to avoid bleeding, for that often occurs. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  This windy hernia—I have seen no one dare treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a pouch, that is, that the vessels should be gently ligated below after incising them.
Then incise in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall away, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it does descend, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the tear in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum; and this happens from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with the cautery in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia transversely to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum which follows it is exposed, then you take a probe and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and push it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the end of the probe, and you adhere one to the other with the suture.
Then you remove the probe, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
Then, if the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
Cauterization is better in this location because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  Whoever wishes to treat it should place the patient on his back.
And he cuts away all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then brings the edges together. And if you wish, first suture the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, making sure the sutures are secure, then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter Sixty-Nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our law.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that the physician may know about it if he is asked, and know how to treat someone who has undergone it; and the other, because we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  That which is done by crushing is done in this way: we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften, and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them off after you have peeled them, and leave nothing of the membranes except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal may still desire mating. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types: one is that which appears in the area behind the scrotum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum's skin.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male testicles protruding outward; one of them is like a man's penis, and the two are like testicles.  And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is gone, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows, which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it, nor is there benefit from seeing it.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural state to the point of being unsightly and unpleasant. In some women, it may even become large, like that of men, and lead to sexual intercourse.
It is necessary to grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with forceps and cut it, but do not go too far in cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail disease."  It should be cut like the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vagina is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be congenital or acquired.  The acquired type results from a pre-existing condition, such as an excess growth of flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane. This may be located in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may even prevent menstruation.  This is determined by examination by a midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and near the surface, then if it is not apparent, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from a thin membrane and is near the labia, then proceed to pierce it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread apart. Then, she forcefully stretches the labia until she pierces the thin membrane and opens the obstruction. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the area. The woman has intercourse every day so that the area does not close up again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, then it should be incised with a wide scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from an excess growth of flesh, then suspend it with hooks and cut it.  You should have styptics at hand which do not cause stinging, such as oak galls, acacia gum, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly; keep it in place for several days. Use a wick of dry linen, then treat it with the usual treatment for healing until it is cured.  Another fleshy growth may appear in the uterus; it should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor. A cancerous tumor which is in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules which appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, it is a swelling of the mouths of the veins until much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids are present for some time they become warts.  Their types and signs have been mentioned in the classification; we will mention them here.
What of them accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are in the depth of the uterus and do not appear to the touch, have no treatment with iron; and that which is of them at the mouth of the uterus, which is felt, is what is treated.
Then it is necessary that the woman enter a cool house, then she should stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots; then she should apply to them, when the blood flows, one of the styptics which stops the blood without cauterization.
Such as acacia,  [a type of]  Sumac, frankincense, and the like; then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, then seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and the like; then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the blood stops, otherwise apply cupping glasses to the loins and breasts without restriction; then take wool, dip it in the juice of the plant *tharthith* or the juice of *Plantago* or the juice of the branches of *al-‘illiq* with a gallnut potion and apply it to the place.
If the swelling subsides, then treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of the *hashā* [a type of insect], rough in appearance; it should be cut off as much as appears, according to what I mentioned concerning warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the bursting of an abscess which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, hardened tumors, ulcers, fistulas, phagedena, sinuses, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors. I have mentioned all of these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this chapter the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which gather for a period, how its bursting with iron is to be done.  It should be examined; if the pain of the tumor is severe in its beginning, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation and fever,
and you see it red when touched, do not hasten to burst it, and treat it with what assists in ripening, until these symptoms subside. Then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, and let her thighs be spread apart, and let her arms be under her knees, and bind them with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the womb is opened, the image of which comes later. If you wish to use this instrument, you should estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest that which enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
It will harm the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus.  The screw which runs in the instrument should be on the upper side.
And you hold the instrument, and the assistant turns the screw until the cervix is opened. If the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it should be incised at its head with a broad scalpel. When all the matter has been evacuated,
a soft pledget soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent should be placed in the wound. The pledget should come out from the incision in the cervix. Clean wool soaked in water in which alum has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment which is suitable for this until it heals.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which liquorice root or long zedoary has been boiled, then return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
It is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated as we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural way.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth.  Its signs are: if you see the woman straining downwards and longing to breathe the air, and what she suffers from the labour becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is quickened. Know that this birth is in the natural way, and its emergence is head-first, with the placenta either with it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head-first, the placenta descends with it and cleanses itself of those impurities well.  Anything other than this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge feet-first, or its hands and feet may emerge before its head.
Or one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it turns onto its back, and so on from the blameworthy forms.  The midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I will explain each form and how to manage it, so that you may learn from it and understand it. If the fetus emerges head-first – the natural emergence – and this becomes difficult for the woman, and labor is hard for her, and you see her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to hold her.
And pack her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and incises the placenta with it, or incises it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirāj ointment, then after the injection, she should instruct her to cough and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, and hold her mouth and nose; then the fetus will emerge immediately. If the fetus's hands emerge first, then they should be gently returned little by little.
If they do not manage to enter, place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately; then he will emerge.
