The emergence of the fetus with its feet: If the fetus emerges with its feet, then they should be pushed upwards, then the fetus should be turned little by little until it is straightened, then take one of its legs and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and make her sneeze with a sternutatory, then he will emerge. If he does not emerge as we have described, then return the fetus little by little until it is in its natural position; then it will emerge smoothly. If it is prevented by all that we have described,
Take some henbane saliva, fenugreek saliva, shirāj ointment, and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the ankles.
If you see that her lower parts have softened, make a pessary for her from myrrh and let her hold it.  If the pessary is retained for an hour, seat her on a stool, then make her sneeze, close her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen; then the fetus will emerge immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands:
If it emerges in this manner, gradually introduce your hands. Then the woman should lie on her back, her hips hanging downwards while lying on a bed, and clasp her hands; then press gently above her abdomen. Then remove the cushions from under her legs so that her lower body is raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then press gently above the ankles until the fetus ascends, then the midwife inserts her hand and adjusts the fetus little by little.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The emergence of the fetus transversely, one of its hands hanging down: try to return its hand.  If you are unable to return it, have the woman stand and walk; if she is unable to walk, let her lie down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice that I described from the leeches. If labor begins, seat her on a stool, then work to reposition the arm and straighten the fetus to its natural position, make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it emerges.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, its face towards its mother's back: the midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and straighten it gently.  Once straightened, apply what we described of the ointment and poultices to its head.
Then tell her to move little by little. If she does that, seat her on the stool and tell her to lean to her right side. Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with a sternutation powder; then it will emerge.  The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture. If it is attached to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the navel lest it suffocate and die, then push the fetus inward until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly; then it will emerge easily. The emergence of twins or multiple fetuses: Know that twins are frequently born, and three or four may be born and live, although this is rare; as for five, it is something outside of nature, they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by one placenta, and they are separated by a membrane barrier between them, attached to the navel of each one.  And they may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, and they may emerge in a blameworthy manner, as we have described.
If one of them is difficult at birth and becomes as we have said, in unnatural forms, then treat it as we have described. And let the midwife be gentle and do what she does gently and slowly, and adapt to every form with whatever expedient presents itself to her that leads to safety.
Mention of what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted: One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb.  It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the Almighty and Knowing One, all of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may befall her someday, and she may meet it with what is proper.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
In the extraction of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to use your hand, then you should look: if the woman is healthy and strong, and has none of the symptoms from which harm is feared, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be inclined downwards and her legs raised, and an attendant should support her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is drawn out.
Then moisten her uterine orifice with moist ointments, along with henbane saliva and fenugreek and flaxseed. Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and salivas, then gently insert it into the uterus, then seek with it a place to insert the hooks into the fetus, and the appropriate places for that.
Look, if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, then insert the hooks into its eyes, or into the nape of its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its chin or collarbone, or into the areas near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
But if its descent is feet first, then [insert the hooks] into the groin and the middle ribs.  It is necessary that you hold the hook with the right hand and place its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and insert a hook into some of those places which we mentioned.
Until you reach something firm, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not lean to one side. Then pull with a steady pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but moved to every direction.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a molar when it is extracted.  It is necessary during this that the pull be relaxed; if it is obstructed from one side, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to move what is obstructed.  If some of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
She should raise it a little; do this until the whole fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it, then pull it until, when it has completely emerged, you cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Thus it is necessary to act in the other hand and in the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences pressure in exiting, or if there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked scalpel between the fingers.
And split the head with it so that the water emerges, or puncture it with the instrument called the perforator, whose image comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter that follows this.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, then it is necessary to split the skull or perforate it with the perforator, as we said, then remove the bones with forceps. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let it be split so that the moisture in the chest is released.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and it is not possible to remove it, cut it as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains a tumor, it is necessary to incise it until the moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is in a feet-first position, then pulling it out is easy and straightening it to the mouth of the womb is simple.  If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and split the abdomen or chest until what is inside flows out.
If all the limbs are extracted and the head retreats and is retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the womb and search for the head with it and pull it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb. Then let her insert a hook or two hooks and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has contracted due to a hot swelling affecting it, then it should not be forced. Instead, it is necessary to use the pouring of moist, fatty substances, and to use poultices, and to sit in waters that relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, let what we mentioned for a living fetus be used. If this is not possible, let it be cut up and removed.  It is necessary not to leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot tumors that occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have indeed seen a woman who had conceived and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a swelling appeared in her uterus and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close.  So I applied some strong, drawing ointments to it.
Then a bone came out of the place. Then after some days, another bone came out. I wondered at this, since the abdomen is a place where there are no bones. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted from it many bones from the skull of the fetus.
I continued this until I had extracted many bones, and the woman was in the best of health. She lived like this for a time, with a little pus discharging from the place.  And I have only brought this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are images of the instruments needed for extracting the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is a binding instrument which evens out books; it has two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binding instrument and should be made either of ebony or boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width is about a finger, and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same wood, which are fastened in them, their length being half a span or a little more, and their width about two fingers or a little more, and these two guides...
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it by turning the screws; an image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter:
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guides at its end, as you see; the length of each guide is about a span, and their width is two fingers; and if you wish to open the womb with it...
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two guides together into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, according to how much you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants.  An image of another screw mentioned by the ancients:
Image of the instrument which pushes out the fetus:
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is extracted.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, you cut and crush with it.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it. Image of a hook with two barbs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his standing among people. Therefore, do not scorn any instrument that you have prepared, for there will certainly be a need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
On the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the woman to hold herself, then cause her to sneeze with a sternutatory, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as rue, henbane, dill, chamomile, wormwood, vervain, and centaury – all these medicaments or some of them – and immerse them in water, and place them over the fire, then place a silver tube over the hole in the pot’s lid, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then cause her to sneeze as we said; then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in olive oil or mallow juice.
Then she should insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta; if she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out little by little.  If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described, until, when you find the placenta, you pull it out little by little, carefully.
Lest the womb fall out upon strong pulling; rather, it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling should be increased; then it will yield and be freed from its adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out by all that we have described, avoid violence in pulling again; but it should be tied, that which has come out of it to the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the four-ingredient ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out; however, if it rots, a bad smell will ascend from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient; therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried fumigation with rue and dried figs.  Image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated in cases of retained menses, placenta, and the like:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper; the thin end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the embers; it is held until that incense is gone, and then another is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their anus imperforate; a thin membrane has closed it.  The midwife should perforate this membrane with her finger; otherwise, she should incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should place on it wool soaked in syrup and oil, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close up, she should place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it whenever the child wishes to defecate.
The anus may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor.  This adhesion should be incised, and then treated as described above, with medication and the tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the anus, externally or internally in the space from one of the sides. The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white moisture or thin pus flows from it. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the rectum and to the intestine, and others may not penetrate. Those which penetrate may be recognized by the passage of stool and flatus from them when the patient uses the privy.
And sometimes worms emerge from them.  There may be fistulas which, if they are in the space, penetrate to the bladder and to the urethra. And there may be some which penetrate to the hip joint and to the sacrum.  And a fistula which penetrates to the rectum is also known from one which does not penetrate.
That you insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula, of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, then insert into it a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of stool, nor flatus, nor worms, as we said, emerges from the opening, then know that it is not penetrating, and proceed to treatment.
And if the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary passage, its indication is the emergence of urine from it, and the prevention of the place from healing with medications. And as for whether it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, its sign is the arrival of the probe there.
If it is not tortuous to the bone, and there is pain in that direction, and pus constantly emerges from it, and no treatment is effective, nor does it heal with ointment, and all these penetrating fistulas, then there is absolutely no cure for them.
And its treatment is a vain effort for those who attempt it among ignorant physicians. As for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, healing is hoped for with cautery, as I describe and as I have experienced. That is, that you place the patient between your hands on his back.
And raise his legs upwards, and his thighs inclined towards his abdomen. Then insert the copper or lead probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, exposed, without feeling between your finger and it any membrane or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself with it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
And it has been said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments from which benefit is hoped for, is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that superfluous, matted flesh resembling a bird's quill has been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until you remove the burned flesh. Then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and there was between it and the probe a thick barrier of flesh or membrane, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin at the beginning of the fistula. And as you pass with the incision with the probe in the fistula, until you reach with the incision where the tip of the probe ends, the probe will become loose and fall out. Then cleanse that matted flesh resembling a bird's quill, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that superfluous flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the area suppurates and that burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that generate firm flesh.
These are medicines which have an astringent and drying effect; for when it is closed, it is healed and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a partition of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then the action is only through hope and expectation; that is, to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom, so that you can easily insert the lint and treat it closely.  Do not extend the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient involuntary evacuation of stool. Then treat it as we mentioned, for perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that partition and perforate it after incising it near the anus as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then clean out all the hardened flesh in the incision as far as you can thoroughly.
Then suture the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, leaving the fistula open inside the anus, which is easier for the patient. The fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on one side of the anus towards the surface of the body with the skin and the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end like a shoemaker's needle, in this manner:.
And insert a twisted thread of five or so threads into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom, if it is penetrating the margin of the anus from the inside nearby.
Then bring the thread out through that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread, and bring both ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the thread is severed and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that on account of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then treat it as we have described until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised: its hook is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, and their excision and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And there may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, in most cases, less moist; a yellow fluid or a little blood constantly flows from them, and they are the color of the body.
And the treatment of those which are inside the rectum is that you order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the excrescences appear to you. Then you quickly seize them with hooks or grasp them with your fingernail and then cut them at their bases, if the hooks do not hold them because of their moisture and laxity.
Then scrape it with a rough cloth or a piece of a rasp or pull it with your fingers, then cut it, then sprinkle on it after cutting some sharp powders to act in place of cautery, or cauterize it as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, then inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and to quickly come out when the patient strains. As for the excrescences outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
And that is that you seize it with your fingernail or hook it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron, it is necessary that you use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pass the needle through its base on the other side, and twist the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle.  Do this to all the excrescences and leave one unligated.
so that the excess blood flows from it, then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or kypriot oil, and place it on the rectum and tell the patient to remain still, then leave him until it falls off. And when the excrescences fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from the dryness of the feces and the retention of the temperament. If it becomes chronic and medicine does not work for it, then it is necessary to scrape it with the blade of a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer which prevents its healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than the first time until it bleeds well and is peeled, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts and the ant.
A nail is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail. It occurs on the whole body, especially on the soles of the feet and the fingers, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the nail and grasp it with a hook or a forceps and pull it out from its root, then treat it. And if you wish, cauterize it as mentioned in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, matted, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it as mentioned before, with fire or with a caustic medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument which I have mentioned in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant, it is also a small, matted, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, going very deep, and if it is affected by cold, one feels in it something like the sting of ants.
It occurs on the whole body, but mostly on the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from a goose quill or a strong eagle feather and place this tube on the ant until the circle of the tube surrounds it from all sides, then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the ant.
And make it go deep into the flesh, and the ant will be removed from its root. And if you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube, towards the tip, should be solid and wired so that it is easy for the fingers to grasp and twist it. And if you wish, cauterize the ant as previously mentioned in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter eighty-three.
On the forms of instruments used for injections in rectal diseases, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or china, or cast or hammered copper.  These instruments may be made small or large, according to the users. Those used in the treatment of small children, whose rectums are narrow or sore, should be very gentle.  Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is attached, is wide in this manner. It has a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see. Its lower end, which enters the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and delicate. On one side there are two holes, and on the other side, one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a probe, or slightly thicker. The bladder, in which the medicine is placed, is made from an animal bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small bag, its diameter being a span.
It is pierced with many holes in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then, a tight thread of twisted yarn, made of ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes and the bladder is gathered like a pouch. When the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly bound in the head of the syringe between the stones, then injected. This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the hole:
The method of injection is to fill the skin or bladder with the warmed medicine, to a degree tolerable to the patient. Be careful that it is not too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
A cloth or blanket is placed beneath him. Then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or something similar. Then it is gently inserted into the rectum.  Then an assistant takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it forcefully until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him hold it as long as he can. If he sleeps with it all night, it will be more beneficial. If it is injected for diarrhea and its expulsion is hastened, repeat the injection until he recovers.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the rectum, the syringe should be very soft and smooth, like those used for children. This is an image of a soft syringe with its leather covering:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of wounds.
I mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterization, and I will now describe its treatment by hand and by medication completely. I say that wounds vary according to the instrument that causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as a blow from a stone, or a cut from a sword or knife, or a stab from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to their location on the body, there is the wound that occurs on the head, or neck, or chest, or abdomen, or liver, and so on from the organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds, especially because the compound ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow from a stone or the like, and only the skin has been split, and the wound is large and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and incise the cephalic vein in the appropriate place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little; and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side to the injured part.
And warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and coarse foods; and if something prevents him from bloodletting, let him reduce food and not approach meats or drinks, and apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a styptic drink.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the powder which I will describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals; and if the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like, and its edges do not come together by themselves, then bring them together by suturing, as I describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs, and its attachment is only by a small piece, cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines which produce firm flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to nourish himself with foods that have strength, such as heads and brains and the like.
If decay occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut away all that is decayed and throw it away, then treat it; and if in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding and the bleeding does not stop with medication, search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, sever it with the scalpel and tie it; and if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned is not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
before the air changes it.  The preparation of the powder is: take one part frankincense, two parts  styrax, and three parts of quicklime or unslaked lime.  Grind everything, sift it, and fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood, then bind it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not come away from it until it heals.  The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and styrax.
This action may also be performed with cobwebs in many large wounds if its application and binding are skillfully done, and the wounds are fresh and bleeding, provided the air has not changed them, but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer.
then there is no point in applying this powder; treat it as mentioned before, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until it discharges pus, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is slight, draw it with a splint. I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
And nothing cold should be applied to the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of the organs.  What you treat it with should be from soft medicines, such as burnt alum washed many times with fresh water, moistened with oil, or rose oil and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments; but if the wound is large, use suture or approximate its edges with pledgets, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound is deep and a cavity forms at its bottom where pus collects, open it at its lowest point. If an artery is severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, and the trachea is unharmed, unite the edges of the skin by suture over the windpipe; do not touch the windpipe itself, but straighten it and restore it to its natural shape, then bind it tightly and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it; if there are bone splinters, extract them all, or as many as possible.  What you cannot remove, leave until the wound suppurates, heals, and their removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone in the wound becomes necrotic and fistulous, treat it as described in its chapter, on the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife, and you see that it is deep, observe whether air escapes from it when the patient breathes; if so, know that it is a mortal wound. If it is not deep and is fresh with blood, do not at first apply powder to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depths.
This will cause it to spread to the heart and kill the patient. But apply an absorbing ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place an old piece of cotton wool in the mouth of the wound to absorb the fluids that exude, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that what collects there may flow out.  If three days or more have passed since the injury, and the patient has experienced no convulsions,  faintness, or shortness of breath, and you see that his condition is otherwise good, know that the wound is safe; treat it with cautery and other therapies until it heals.
If its observation is difficult and the pus flows constantly, know that it has become fistulous, and treat it according to its chapter.  If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the stitching described above.
If it is large, cauterize it; if small, use powder. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and immediately remove these fragments if they are free; if not free, leave them until...
the wound becomes septic, for then their removal will be easier. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the rules we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which affects the abdomen may be large, or small, or medium.  A large wound may allow the protrusion of one or several intestines, and their replacement and suturing will be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way, necessitating more difficult replacement.  If the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its replacement difficult.  Therefore, the medium-sized wound is preferable, because the replacement of the intestine is not as difficult as in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types.  Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing other wounds of the body. The other two sutures are specific, used for all wounds, but they are most especially used for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine has protruded and its replacement is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been affected by swelling due to the coldness of the air.
If this is so, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a damp cloth into lukewarm water alone, or water in which anise, rue, and spikenard have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the swelling subsides.
A medicament with astringent properties may also do this; it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling. If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which alum or pomegranate has been boiled, for it facilitates its return with the least effort.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used to incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision. Incision is only resorted to after necessity and the failure of other methods. This is an image of the instrument:
One side is curved and sharp, the other is blunt. The thin end is not as sharp as a lancet, but somewhat blunt. It is an instrument similar to a small spatula, as you see. If the wound has widened and the intestine has entered, it should be returned to its natural shape.
And its proper position, if you can achieve it, is best. But if the wound is large and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always make the side with the injury higher than the other.  This should also be used in large and moderate wounds.
As for small wounds, position him as best you can. Then have a skilled assistant ready to hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing its edges together, and then revealing it to the one performing the suturing, little by little.
This is the description of a general stitch: take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space the width of a little finger from the edge of the wound, and insert a needle without thread into the edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside, until you bring it out on that side.  You have now gathered the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then, tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space the width of a finger again, and insert another needle, and tie it with thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as necessary until you have completed the entire circumference of the wound.  Let the needles be of medium thickness.
For very fine needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, they should be of medium thickness, and the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals. Do not space the stitches too far apart either, lest the wound fail to unite. Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient when he sleeps, and make him supports of linen strips on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, may suffice for suturing them.
As for the method of suturing the second type, the common one, it is to bring together the four edges, that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, at once with a needle with a twisted thread, moderate in thinness and thickness. Then, when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle to the same side from which you began.
So that the thread falls interwoven from above the wound, so that the stitching is according to the stitching of the purse with which goods are tied. And make between each stitch a distance the thickness of the little finger. This stitching is known to everyone.
With this suture, I stitched a wound which appeared on a man in his abdomen. He had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was more than an inch long. About two inches of the middle intestine had protruded, and the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I replaced it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered. He lived many years thereafter, managing all his affairs. The doctors had judged that he would never recover.
And it is strange that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines. I used to apply worn cotton to the wound twice a day and treat it by washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to the words of Galen. It is that you begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside.  When the needle has passed through the skin and the muscle going straight along the whole length of the belly.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which I inserted the needle, and I passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. When I had passed it through, I passed it again through this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it also through the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until you pass it completely through.
Then begin also from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out through the skin near it, then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out through the skin on its side.  Do this again and again, one time after another, until you have sewn up the whole wound in that way. The general description of this stitching is that you stitch it like the stitching of furs to furs.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special stitching which Galen also mentioned, these are his exact words.
And that is to sew it in the way some physicians sew it, by joining each part to its counterpart similar to it in nature, so that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the muscle to its other edge, and that is as I will describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the edge of the muscle nearest to you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the edge of the other muscle which is on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the easy, common stitching, which is the stitching previously mentioned. These are Galen's exact words. And some have said that the stitching of the abdomen may be straightened with two needles. That is to insert one thread into them both, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, then pass it from your side, according to the stitching of the saddlers.
And know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestines and stitch it and tighten it, then apply to it the medicated lint.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out and the peritoneum and muscle unite, then treat it as you treat other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from when it begins to suppurate, old cotton, and change it twice a day as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple.  If you fear that the wound will affect the principal organs with pain,
then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines—and this often occurs—then inject him with a lukewarm black astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what was thick from the intestines is easier to heal, and what was thin from it is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all. This is because of the abundance of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve.  And as for if what emerged from the wound was omentum and you apprehended it fresh, then return it in the same way you would return the intestine.
If a period of time has passed and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place that has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for there are veins and arteries in the omentum, then cut below that ligature.
And make the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to remove and extract it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.  [The text] mentioned the wound which occurs in the intestine. But if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small,
it is possible that it will close in some people, for I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach. The wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged. So I treated him although I did not have hope for his recovery. So I continued to treat him gently until he healed and the place united. When I saw the place had united, I feared for the patient that a bad accident might occur in his abdomen, but no bad accident occurred to him at all, and he remained in the best of his condition. He eats and drinks properly, engages in sexual intercourse, enters the bath, and exercises in his service. Some experienced people have mentioned that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And that is to take large-headed ants, then bring the edges of the wound together, then place one of them, with its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it has seized and tightened its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and will not come undone. Then place another ant near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you dress it and stitch the wound, for those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm at all occurs to the patient.  It is also possible to suture the intestine with fine thread.
Which is withdrawn from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it is inserted into a needle. That is to take the end of this thread from the intestine, then draw it out gently, then tie to its end a fine twisted linen thread, then insert that thread into the needle. And in it is the intestinal thread, with which the intestine is sutured, then it is returned to the abdomen. This type of suturing with ants and intestine is only a matter of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter eighty-six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and protracted and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever member it may be, a fistula; and we call it catarrh, and fistula.
It is in reality a hardened, matted, white mass, without pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, always discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture is interrupted; and this moisture may be abundant or little, thick or thin.
And I have already mentioned in the classification, when discussing wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is due to one of nine causes; one of them is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body, or to its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges, which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter which it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a corruption which has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a special characteristic of the region.
Or because it is very dirty and foul; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter which it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a corruption which has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a special characteristic of the region. As occurred in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one or several bones in it. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatment with drugs in the classification.
It is appropriate here that I should inform you of the treatment of fistula and abscess which is done by hand. Know that fistulas may occur in all the members of the body; some of them reach large veins and arteries, or a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib of the ribs, or to a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back, or to a joint of the compound joints, such as the hand and foot; and some of them lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so forth.
Then such fistulas as are of this kind are among the difficult diseases to cure, which do not admit of treatment, and to occupy oneself with them is trouble and ignorance. But if any of the fistulas does not reach these places which I have mentioned, and there is hope and expectation of its cure,
and you wish to arrive at a knowledge of it, take a probe of copper or iron; if the fistula runs straight, explore it with it; but if there is any tortuosity in the fistula, explore it with a slender leaden probe, because lead is soft in its substance.
It yields when entering, and bends towards the tortuosity.  If the fistula has many mouths, and you cannot ascertain them with the probe, inject one of its mouths; then the moisture which you inject will pass towards the other mouths and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in whatever way you can, to find out if there is bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is one fistula with many mouths; ascertain all this as far as you can, by inquiring of the patient and by noting the pain when you touch the place.
and similar signs and causes of the swelling. When you have ascertained all this with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  And that is, that you should consider whether, if...
the fistula is superficial and near, or in a sound place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I have mentioned to you, then lay open the fistula as I have described, and remove what is in it of adhesion and corrupted and redundant flesh.
And whatever grows in its mouth of flesh or wart, and the like, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, you should incise it deeply as far as you can, and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use a pledget soaked in strong medicines and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument; do this repeatedly until the strong medicine eats away all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then dress it with ointments which cause healthy flesh to grow until it heals. If it does not heal in this way, cauterize it as previously described.  If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of that, then probe it and incise it as I have told you, unless something prevents you.
a nerve, or a principal member, as we have said. If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness in it, scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will unite it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as before,
know that you have not completely removed the corruption.  Expose everything again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the greatest effort, then dress it. If it heals, well and good; otherwise, know that the corruption is in a deep recess, expose everything again.
and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the greatest effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, pull it out with delicate forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, make every effort to extract them all and do not leave any behind. If something of the ligaments obstructs you, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and flesh, if any are there, and remove the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones at once, apply something to the wound that will cause it to fester, and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones fester.  Keep the wound from closing, the incision that you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments have festered and the bones have become loose, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is a single large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh, and so forth, and only its surface is corrupted, scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption is gone, then dress the wound.  If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to remove it.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. And I tell you of a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to serve as an example and aid to your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause originating within the body, until the matter reached the leg and swelled into a large swelling; there was no external cause. Then time passed, along with the mistakes of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
And much matter flowed from it, and he was treated badly, until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and the body's fluids. A group of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were skilled in the craft of surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in great quantity, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow.  Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found that they led to one another from all sides of the leg. So I began by incising one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone, and I found it corrupted, eaten away, black, putrid, and perforated, until the perforation reached the marrow.  Then I excised what was revealed to me.
And I removed the corrupted bone; and I thought that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and excised, and that I had eradicated it. Then I proceeded to treat the wound with cauterizing medications for a long time, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the corruption connected to the bone. So I excised what was also revealed to me of that corruption. Then I attempted to heal it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut away the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I treated it with medications, and it healed quickly and completely.  This repetition in its operation and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness and his inability to endure, and my fear for his death, because he frequently experienced severe fainting from excessive vomiting.  He recovered a complete recovery.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body was improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in walking, nothing whatsoever harmed him. If a projecting bone in a part of the body is broken,
it should be excised in this manner: you should take a ligature and tie it around the end of the projecting bone, and instruct someone to pull it upwards, and make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, while you expose the flesh from the place you want to excise, lest the saw injure the flesh, and place a firm piece of wood or board beneath the bone from below.
For if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone.  The excision should be slightly above the location of the corruption, lest there be corruption in the cavity of the bone that does not appear on its surface to the senses, thus requiring you to excise it again.
If the bone is corrupted and not projecting, but its parts are connected and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides. Then place the wood underneath, and then excise it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished excising it from one side, excise it from the other side, and let the excision be a little distance from the corruption, as we said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and excise the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no recourse but to remove it. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, treatment is very difficult, but you should peel off the corruption however it appears to you, and remove it and clean it in any way you can.
And by any method that works for you, as long as a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position, arrangement, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and porosity of the bones. Therefore, for each type of work, you should prepare an instrument suitable for that work.
Know that the operations themselves can indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this profession, for this is a sign of mastery of the craft and of having seen many kinds of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I will depict for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
These are examples to follow and measurements by which you can measure other instruments, God willing. Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause.  If you treat that corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing preventing it,
then it is necessary to amputate that limb to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great affliction which is greater than the loss of the limb. A sign of this happening to someone is that the limb turns black, until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after turning black, until that putridity spreads to what is next to that limb and takes hold in the whole body; then hasten to amputate it.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, like a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, or the like.
If the corruption or the sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay, lest the corruption spread to the hand. Likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not delay, lest the corruption spread until it takes hold in the bones of the arm.
Likewise, if it has taken hold in the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption progresses and you see it reaching toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient. Use other treatments as much as possible.
And likewise you do with the leg: if the corruption takes hold in the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints; and if it takes hold in the metatarsus, amputate the whole foot; and if it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to abandon it and leave the patient to death.  The method of amputating or excising a limb is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut and tie another ligature above the place.
One assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards. You then expose the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is revealed, then it is cut or excised.  It is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy area, which would cause the patient excessive pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some styptic powders, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you of an example of a man who experienced this very condition in his leg; that is, blackness appeared in his leg along with burning resembling fire.
This corruption first appeared in his toe, until it consumed the whole leg.  The man, upon seeing the corruption spreading in his limb, along with the intense pain and burning, amputated it himself at the joint and recovered.
After a long time, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me, and I tried to stop its spread with the medications I applied to his hand after cleansing his body.  However, the corruption did not stop and continued to spread into the other finger.
Until the corruption consumed his hand, and he asked me to amputate it. I refused, hoping to stop the spread of the corruption, and I also feared for his life if I amputated his hand, as his strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then I heard that he had amputated his entire hand and recovered. I have recounted this story to help in cases of this type of illness and to serve as a guide to be followed.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medication.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy limbs and the swelling persists for a long time, then bursts or is lanced, and all the matter within it comes out, leaving the place empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, thinned, and its corruption has not affected bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects any of these members.
Then it is called a sinus or fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin completely, especially if it has thinned and become like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and its corruption has not reached that extent, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection.  This is to look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, and bind its mouth, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing. Do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the stench is gone.  And it may be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you. This is to take the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pour water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been cleansed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and syrup closes the fistula, especially if the syrup has a binding and drying property.  When you have done this several times and are certain that the fistula’s corruption has gone, then inject it with something that will cause flesh to grow in it.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent syrup, or injecting it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have established in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the fistula is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to enter,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  And similarly, if the mouth is also wide, bring its lips together with sutures and leave only enough space for the injection tube to enter, no more.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, make an incision in the lowest part of it so that the pus will flow downwards; for if pus is retained in the depth of the fistula, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows easily from it, as seems best to you, so that no pus at all is retained in it.
As for the warming plasters which are applied externally to the fistula, and the method of applying them, take one of the warming ointments similar to what you want, and spread it on a linen cloth, and let the cloth be large enough to cover the entire fistula. Then cut with scissors, opposite the mouth of the fistula, a hole slightly larger than the mouth of the fistula. Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, the size of the hole, and place it there, some distance from the mouth of the fistula, so that the pus may easily escape from it, and do not remove the larger cloth until afterwards.
several days; but the one on the mouth of the fistula, you remove at all times so that what has collected there of pus may come out, and so that you may know whether what flows from it of pus is much or little, mature or immature.
And with this also, examine the place of the fistula itself; does its owner feel pain in it or not, and is there swelling in it or not? If your treatment continues thus, and you see that the matter which comes out is slight at the mouth of the fistula,
know that the fistula has adhered or is close to adhering; then dissolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you have dissolved the wound, change the cloths for others and renew the ointment. If after a long time thin pus comes out from the fistula, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If after many days immature pus comes out from the fistula, know that the fistula has not adhered. If the fistula is slow to adhere,
and its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medicines, and let them be medicines which are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like palm ointment if there has been added to it an excess of extract of henbane. And the most effective thing with which it may be treated is that palm ointment be taken.
and it is dissolved with rose oil, and old wine of moderate consistency is sprinkled on it, then you knead it with it and use it. He may do the same thing if palm ointment is not available to you: honey, if you cook it until it thickens, and use it, or take myrrh, aloes, and costus, and crush all together and sprinkle it on honey which is over the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it on the fistula, or it may be spread with the honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle the medicaments on it and place the cloths on it and bind it.
And there may be used in such a case long rhubarb, the root of the asphodel, pounded costus, and centaury, singly or compounded; crush them, sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey which I have described and use it. If the matter of the fistula is prolonged and it does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And it has affected the bones, or the other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment of the fistula, as has been described.
Chapter eighty-nine.
In the treatment of the felon and the bruised nail, and the cutting off of the superfluous finger, and the separation of the adhesion of fingers.
A felon is an excess of flesh which grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and perhaps it grows in the other fingers.  If its matter is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, it swells with a hot swelling.
Or it becomes corrupt and foul until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps it corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul smell, and the tip of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes livid. Then if you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and your treatment does not work,
it is necessary to cut with iron all the excess which grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards; for cauterization in this is very beneficial. And as for if the bone is sound and the nail also is sound,
and the corner containing the nail has drawn the flesh inwards and caused it to be pinched and injured, then it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the corner of the nail which pinches the flesh and raise it upwards, and cut that flesh gently.
And you place on what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing corrosive medicines until it all goes away, then treat it with ointments until it heals. And as for if the corruption has affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and take it out, for it does not heal at all as long as there is corrupt bone in it.
Then if you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints as mentioned before, then treat it until it heals.  If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then you incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above to below, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for by that you cause the patient severe pain and it becomes a cause for the growth of excess flesh in the place, then treat the place with what soothes the pain.
As for the extra finger which grows in some people's hands, perhaps it is entirely fleshy, and perhaps it has bones in it, and perhaps it has a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the finger joints; those which grow in some of the finger joints do not move, and those which grow at the finger joint may move. As for that which is fleshy only, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its base with a broad scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some to others, that often occurs, and that is from what a person is born with, and it is when a wound heals or a burn from fire and the like, then it is necessary to incise that adhesion.
until the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a pledget or a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil, lest it adhere quickly, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
And likewise, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, then incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting off haemorrhoids.
Haemorrhoids are twisted, thick, swollen veins filled with dark fluid which occur in most parts of the body, and most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens and those who work as porters, so firstly it is necessary to use rubbing of the body with black hellebore repeatedly with strong rubbing.
Then bleed the patient in the basilica vein, and as for its treatment with an iron instrument, it is of two types: one is to incise and remove the black blood; and the other is to extract the vein and remove it entirely.  As for its incision, it is in this manner: first bathe the leg with warm water well.
Until the thick, cloudy blood is dissolved, then tie the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage, then incise the vein in one place, or in two places, or in three, with a wide incision.
Then draw out the black blood with your hand from the lower leg upwards and from above downwards until you remove the amount of blood that you see as sufficient, or what the patient's strength can tolerate. Then bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat purging and bleeding.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient. As for its extraction, it is in this manner: shave the patient's leg if there is much hair on it, then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and sweats, or he performs vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, until the limb is heated, then incise the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its lower part near the heel. Then open the skin with hooks and strip away the vein from every side until it is exposed to the touch.
And when it appears, you will see it dark red.  And when it is freed from the skin, you will see it also as if it were a tendon. Then insert a probe under it until, when it is raised and comes out from the skin, hang it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then make another incision near that incision, about the width of three fingers.
Then strip the skin from over the vein until it appears, then raise it with the probe as you did and hang it with another hook as you did at first. Then make another incision, or many incisions if you need to.
Then strip it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it and strip it until it comes out from the second incision, then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until when it is all out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and stripping, then insert a needle with strong doubled thread, and tie it and pull it, and insert the probe under it and twist it with your hand to every side until it comes out. And be careful that it does not break; for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to strip it.
And harm will befall the patient from it.  And when you have stripped it all, put wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or olive oil on the place of the incisions, and treat it until it heals. If the varix is tense with convolutions and torsion in directions…
and was not straight as we said, then it is necessary that you incise it at each part of its convolutions and places of torsion, then hang it with hooks until you strip it completely, and be careful when you incise it that you do not cut or wound the vein, for it will be difficult for you to strip it, so guard your effort.
Image of the stripping by which the varix is incised.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it be sharp-pointed lest it wound the vein, and it should be thick, smoothly curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness, but it should have a considerable thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of the internal hemorrhoid.
