This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hejaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren lands with little fertility, and it may develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
As happens with the generation of living creatures and worms inside bodies, and the gourd worm and the worm that develops between the skin and the flesh; and the sign of the beginning of the development of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place suppurates, then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that suppuration.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal; so when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, and tied, and the lead is left hanging from the leg.
So every time something comes out of it, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the rest; and do not cut it from its root before it all comes out, because if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing swelling, infection in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be carefully and gradually pulled out until it is completely removed and nothing of it remains in the body.  From this vein, in some people, something may come out that is five or ten cubits in length.
And it has reached me that it came out of a man to the length of twenty cubits. If it breaks for you while you are treating it, then insert a probe into its hole and thoroughly probe it along the body until all that is in it of matter is emptied, and treat the place with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, and it creates many openings, and from each opening a branch comes out. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and as previously stated.
Chapter ninety-two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that is generated under the ear and is called the cow's ailment.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's ailment, because it frequently affects cows. It is a single small worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh, and it moves in the whole body, ascending and descending.
It is perceived by touch as it moves from one member to another, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and it comes out.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms and snakes and the gourd-like growth in the abdomen.
The harm to be expected from it is that if it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and come out, destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it is moving and apparent to the touch.
Then you should bind above and below it tightly with a bandage, then incise over it and remove it. If it has gone deep into the flesh and you do not find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is its corruption of the eye, as we said.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, bind tightly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it.  The patient should be treated with purgative medicines to cleanse the body of bad, corrupt humors and should avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On the incision for the disease known as the gnawing ulcer.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some parts of the body and then moves from one member to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short while, I saw that swelling move along the ulna like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it moved from one place to another, the pain left that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it spread throughout her body until it reached the other arm.  She then told me that it circulated around her whole body, as I witnessed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one member to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this manner, although I had seen many people whose pain moved from one member to another.  I had never seen it as I saw it in this woman.
I judged that this was only because the woman was a country dweller, with a dry body and visible veins.  Therefore, this moving wind was apparent to the senses. It would not appear in this way in those of comfortable circumstances, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it tightly with a bandage and incise it so that the contained wind may escape, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel and disperse the wind, such as the seed of rue and the seed of  *siqbinj* and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some have four.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; then if an arrow lodges in one of these organs, and the signs of death which I shall describe to you later appear, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most instances. But if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is upon the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, drowsiness, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is upon the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, drowsiness, and dizziness. and redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, mental confusion, vomiting, and sometimes blood comes from the nostrils or ears, and sometimes speech is lost and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel comes from the place of the wound, and a watery substance like the water of flesh flows from it.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs of an arrow if it strikes the heart, and it is near the left breast and you feel it as if it has been driven into something solid and not into something empty,
and perhaps the arrow has a movement resembling the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and coldness of the extremities and a cold sweat and fainting follow; then know that death is inevitably approaching. And a sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the escape of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels which are near the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and desires to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs, and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he sighs and all the members of the shoulders move. And if the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness comes from the wound. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food comes from the wound.
And its condition is evident. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and becomes embedded in it, and something of the excrement or fat or intestine which has been perforated comes from the wound, then there is no hope for its treatment or for the extraction of the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out,
and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain becomes severe for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for the other members, like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, clavicle, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are mostly safe.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve and the arrow is not poisoned. And I inform you of some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce from it your treatment. That is, an arrow once struck a man in the inner canthus of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered and nothing untoward happened to his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had hidden itself, and I grasped only its small point which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite arrows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered and nothing bad happened to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
And it was one with barbs; so I made an incision upon him between the two muscles of the neck, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently manipulated it until I extracted it.  The Christian was saved and recovered. And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in his abdomen, and we estimated that he would die from it.
Then when thirty days or so had passed and nothing changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and maneuvered it and extracted it, and he recovered and no ill effects occurred.  And I saw a man whom an arrow had struck in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman whom an arrow had struck in her abdomen, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained. Nothing changed in her condition, nor did she experience harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man whom an arrow had struck in the face, and the wound had closed, and he experienced little pain from it. And there are many similar cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man, one of the Sultan's guards, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, leaning slightly to the right side.
And the whole arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow had struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. He felt a stinging pain under his ear on the right side of the incision.
I hoped that the stinging pain was from the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a dressing possessing drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrow's location would become apparent, so that I could incise it. But nothing occurred in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that location.
So I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then the wound healed, and the patient remained despondent about its removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me. So I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin tip of the arrow that adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared to us clearly.  And this took about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to removal. So I continued to gently manipulate it and devise methods with various instruments, until one day I grasped it with firm forceps, as shown in the image at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely, without any further harm. And I tell you how to remove some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman measures the much by the little, and the absent by the present, and devises a new work and a new instrument in the case of strange occurrences.
If we descend to this craft, then I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction; and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, and it is pulled out; and if it does not yield to removal from its place of impact, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrifies, so that its removal is facilitated; and likewise if it is embedded in bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, leave it also for days and return to pulling and moving it each day, for it will come out.  And if it does not yield to removal after days, then you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine borer until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull it out and remove it.
If the arrow embedded in the bone of the head has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms I mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after some days.
then persevere in pulling out the arrow.  And if it is very deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, use the borers around the arrow as I described to you, then treat the wound until it heals. And as for if the arrow has disappeared in a place of the body, and is hidden from sensation, then explore it with a probe. And if you feel it, then pull it with some instrument suitable for pulling it.  And if you are unable to reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein there,
then incise it until the wound widens and you can reach the arrow so that you may extract it.  If it has barbs, grasp them and free the flesh embedded in them from every side by every means you can. And persevere, if you are unable to free the flesh, in breaking the barbs and twisting them until you are freed.
And when you attempt to extract the arrow in whatever place it may be, use the turning of your hand with hooks in all directions until you free it. And be extremely gentle, lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out.  And if you are unable to reach it in your time,
leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, then return to it, for it will then be easier.  And if bleeding obstructs you, use what we mentioned of treatment in its chapter. And safeguard yourself from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon, and use skill.
By every means you can free the arrow, and let that be with gentleness and slowness and steadiness as I described to you. And it is necessary for you, when pulling the arrow, to place the patient in the position he was in when the arrow struck, for that is most suitable.
If you cannot do that, then use whatever positions you can. As for the arrow that exits from the opposite side, either something of it will have emerged outwardly, or you will find the tip of the arrow by feeling from the top of the skin nearby and see it protruding.
Then make an incision upon it, and let the incision be of a size sufficient to admit the hooks, then pull it, for it will be easy to remove. And if it sticks in a bone, turn your hand in a circle until the arrow affects the bone and widens for itself, then pull it.
Otherwise, leave it for days, then return to it until it comes out. And if the shaft of the arrow is in it, push it with it. And if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, then push it with an instrument that enters that cavity, for the arrow will easily come out by that. If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut away all the flesh in which the poison has become, if you can do that, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow that has fallen into the chest, or into the abdomen, or into the bladder, or into the flank was near what can be felt with a probe, and you could make an incision upon it, then make an incision and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and take it out.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing, then treat it until it heals.  The picture of the hooks with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp the arrow or something, they do not let go.  And various kinds are made of them, large, small, and medium-sized, all according to the size and smallness of the arrow, and the width and narrowness of the wound.
The image of the hollow pusher:
The image of the solid pusher:
This is solid at the end like an awl, to make it easier to enter the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
On the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to bleed in the body are thirty veins, of which sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the "sensitive" veins; and the two arteries in the temples, which are apparent; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the "visual" veins.
And the vein which is erect in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip—these veins are known as the "jaw veins"—and the two veins beneath the tongue.
As for the veins which are bled in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the "head vein"; and the median, which is the middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the "body vein".
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side, and is also called the axillary; and the common people call it the "belly vein"; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears clearly above the thumb; and the asil.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the nerve vein, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic catarrhs, migraine, and ringworm, and chronic bad sores of the head; and the manner of bleeding them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his head well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their place appear behind the ears in the two low places of the head; then you search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet, which is known as the *nashal*, and you insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone; then you lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and you cut the vein together with the skin, a cleanly cut; and the length of the cut will be the amount of two fingers placed together.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And I have already mentioned in the beginning of the book their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors which descend to the eyes.
And the manner of bleeding them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the two veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulsation becomes apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you lift the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the lancet, the *nashal*, from below and lift the vein upwards and cut it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out the amount of blood according to your need, then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and you place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place on it cotton and a bandage and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals. And I have already mentioned them, and their cutting and cauterization, in the beginning of the book. As for bleeding the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after bleeding the cephalic vein, is for chronic ailments of the face such as ringworm, ulcers, and persistent redness.
And the manner of bleeding it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument which is called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the axe on the vein itself, and you strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the amount you want; then you loosen the constriction of the patient's neck and bind it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide lancet, except that it is not proper that the lancet be sharp-pointed like the other lancets, but rather it should be slightly broad-ended, and you incise it obliquely, because the bone is nearby, and perhaps the lancet will break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples of the eyes, their benefit is for ailments of the eye such as ringworm, redness, and blemishes, and diseases of the face. As for the manner of bleeding them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you bleed them while you are standing over his head, and let the incision be somewhat obliquely lengthwise with a small lancet, slightly broad.
For the location has no flesh in it; for if the lancet is thin-pointed, perhaps it will break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need and you place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then you unbind them. As for bleeding the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and from the diseases of the face like the red eruption which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The manner of bleeding it is that the patient constricts his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you pierce it in the middle of the nasal bone itself, between the nasal septum, straight, because the vein does not appear to the touch there, for the blood emerges immediately.  And it is necessary that you deepen your hand with the lancet a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugulars, the benefit of bleeding them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, like black vitiligo, impetigo, bad ulcers, and sores.  The manner of bleeding them is that the patient constricts beneath them in his neck with a ligature, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient is seated on a chair, then you bleed the vein lengthwise, a somewhat wide bleeding, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see of the need for it, then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the ligature.
And you constrict the two veins with a moderate constriction, lest the patient suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound heals. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of bleeding them, after bleeding the cephalic vein, is that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in the nose and around it.  The manner of bleeding them is that you seat the patient before you and constrict his neck with a turban, then you turn back his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you ascertain them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins, so you cut them with a severed cut. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, aim to cut the largest and most apparent of them.  And likewise, do in the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of bleeding them, after bleeding the cephalic vein, is for the inflammations which are in the throat from a disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth.  The manner of bleeding them is that you seat the patient between your hands, facing the sun, and you raise his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is toward blackness.
Then you bleed them, and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for under them are arteries, so perhaps a flow of blood from those arteries might occur. As for the three veins which are bled in the elbow, they are those which customarily are bled in all people, and their bleeding is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, lancet-shaped or olive-shaped scalpel to the depth, or either by incising with a knife-like scalpel, which is the *nashil* [and this is its picture], a picture of the wide, lancet-shaped scalpel:
It is wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is a picture of the olive-shaped scalpel:
And this scalpel is less wide and thinner at the point, suitable for bleeding thin veins which contain thin, bilious blood. And this is a picture of the *nashil* scalpel:
This is the *nashil*, which is suitable for incising, and there are wide and thin types of it, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also.  And one may infer from this to others, and it is well-known among the artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen.  It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when bleeding it, to be cautious and be on guard against it, for beneath it is an artery; if he makes a mistake and goes too deep with the scalpel, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur; therefore it is necessary that its bleeding should not be with the lancet, but its bleeding should be an incision with the *nashil*. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or bleed in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the *nashil* scalpel as we said. If you wish to bleed it itself, then before tightening the arm it is necessary to feel the place until you recognize the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and incise the vein with a sharp incision with the *nashil* scalpel as we said, and take care that the blow falls away from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon bleeding, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery; then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the blood stops, it will often stop, and tighten the arm.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be in bed and not move for days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and overcomes you, and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly to the place of the vein with a ligature and pledgets until the next day. If the blood stops, well and good; otherwise, treat it with what was previously mentioned of placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature in the arm, know that; as for the median vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the upper head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
It is necessary for the one who bleeds it to be careful in bleeding it, for beneath it is a nerve; if he increases in the depth of the incision and injures the nerve, numbness occurs in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. And this nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and cannot be distinguished, then you should make your incision of it a superficial one and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the head.
And it benefits from diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to bleed it, to make an incision with the olive-shaped scalpel or with the wide, basil-shaped scalpel, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that it is necessary for you, when bleeding, to avoid with the scalpel only the head of the muscle and seek the soft place; and it does not harm if it is not struck at the first stroke to be bled again several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not bled at the first stroke.
But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the method of bleeding, its complications, and what should precede in its treatment, the first of that is that you should know that bleeding is either used only to preserve health.
Or it is used in illnesses.  That which is used to preserve health and prolong it and to guard against the occurrence of illnesses is that bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median, and the basilic, and that bleeding should be at the beginning of spring if the signs of plethora appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for the bleeding which is used in illnesses, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need demand it, by night or by day.
And at every hour and at every time.  Children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years, nor should old men who have passed sixty years be bled.  If someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever.
He should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained excrement, lest putrid waste be drawn into the veins from the bowels during bleeding, harming the principal organs.  And do not bleed the anxious, nor the drunkard, nor the intoxicated until that passes from them.
And bleeding should also be avoided after fever, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything which weakens the strength, whether physical or psychological. Then look to thinning the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and medicines, if he is able to do so. Then he should enter the bath, if nothing prevents him, or do some exercise, so that the blood is thinned, and make his bleeding in the middle of the day, as we said, and he should aim to free his chest that day from all bad psychological conditions.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical conditions like excessive exertion and strain, sexual intercourse, and the like.  And let him prepare for his session the things which people are accustomed to using, such as various perfumes and fragrant herbs, entertainments, and the like, each person according to what he is able.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm, and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the fillet and twists it two or three times. Let the tightening be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is towards relaxation, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after tightening, it is necessary that the patient rubs both his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then places the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself, a little below the place he wants to bleed, lest the vein slip and he avoids the incision. Because some veins you find are like a tendon, they slip away during phlebotomy, and some are full of wind.  When you place the lancet on them, they sink below the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it does open it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and deliberate in all these matters. Then he lowers the lancet. If the vein opens on that attempt, well and good; otherwise, he repeats it again a little below or above that place quickly, if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is distressed, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, for it may cause a hot swelling, and do not let him enter the bath. Then he may repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet to the same hole gently and in a straight line, and increase the opening a little, and do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
But if you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Put something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood and it is better in this case than oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac and the schechnaia may also do this if one of them is placed on the site; if the blood is thick, it will become thin and dissolve.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy—and this often happens, especially to someone who has only been phlebotomized once or if the opening of the vein was small—act quickly and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour; then it will resolve.
And it is necessary to do this after the blood has completely exited the vein itself or from another vein. If something black or green remains in the place after several days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloe and myrrh dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the plantain and the like.  A swelling and a protrusion often occur when phlebotomizing the basilica vein. Place your hand on it; if you find it soft when you touch it, then it is a benign protrusion, and beware of placing anything we have mentioned on it.
For it may bleed arterial blood; but bandage it with something astringent to solidify the area, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. It is necessary to remove blood from each person according to his strength and the color that predominates in his blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red; and likewise if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is gone.  It is necessary for someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed at once…
…to widen the vein incision and the lancet should be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite is true. It is necessary to remove it in several times, and the opening should be narrow. The best thing to use in phlebotomy is something sharp, well-sharpened, incised, not pierced.
This type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; it is better and safer than phlebotomy across or lengthwise. If someone usually faints during phlebotomy, it is necessary to give him something to eat before phlebotomy, bread soaked in pomegranate juice or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is removed from him three or four times, if he has a cold temperament, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in hydromel or in honey-sweetened drink with herbs or in a fragrant rose-scented drink.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and its cause is the loss of a large amount of blood, then he should be given meat broth and a thin fragrant drink, and he should use aromatherapy with rosewater, and rub his chest with it, and he should use everything else we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which results from vomiting. As for someone who wants to relieve his arm and remove blood a second time, it is necessary for someone whose phlebotomy was for much evacuation and whose strength is weak, that the blood is removed little by little, according to his strength, on consecutive days.
As for someone who wants to relieve his arm and remove blood a second time and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy. As for someone who wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it has inclined, it is necessary to relieve him on the second or third day.
As for someone whose body contains much blood, which has become hot and intense and caused fever, it is necessary to remove blood from him at once, and to remove a large amount, and to widen the opening until fainting occurs, after having observed all the conditions of phlebotomy. And that you place your hand on his pulse when the blood flows, lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you want to open the arm and remove blood a second time.
And if the mouth of the vein is closed and the exit of blood is difficult, do not prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you phlebotomize it a second time, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply to it something of salt dissolved in water, or apply to it something of theriac or  schexnaia, and prick it gently until the blood comes out.  If the vein has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity demands removing blood a second time, then either phlebotomize above that place, or phlebotomize in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomizing the basilic vein, it is phlebotomized instead of the cephalic and basilic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of phlebotomy is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature slightly, a moderate tightening.
Then phlebotomize the vein at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be slightly above the joint of the hand. If the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water.  The use of hot water to facilitate the flow of blood is most often in winter. Phlebotomy of this vein is safest of all veins. Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is neither a major vein nor a nerve beneath it. As for phlebotomizing the vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. Its method of phlebotomy is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then phlebotomize it at a slight angle.  Even if you cut it completely, it will not harm him at all. Be careful not to thrust your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, and the place is devoid of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it so the blood flows in it. For if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and prevent its flow. When you have removed the amount of blood needed, place ointment and salt on the vein lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of phlebotomizing from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen.  You do the same in its phlebotomy as you did in the second, exactly. As for phlebotomizing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for the diseases in the lower body, such as uterine ailments.
And the retention of menses, and kidney diseases, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases. Its method of phlebotomy is that he puts his leg in hot water and rubs it until the veins swell, then you tie above the ankle joint with a ligature; the vein is located at the heel, on the surface towards the big toe.
And many branches extend from it on the surface of the foot. Therefore, phlebotomize in its widest branch, or at the heel where it converges; this is best and safest. If you phlebotomize it on the surface of the foot, be careful of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision as if you intend to amputate it, and let the scalpel be sharp. If the exit of blood is difficult, let him return his leg to the hot water, and leave the blood to flow in it until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein in the first attempt,
let him repeat the phlebotomy a little higher. For the location is safe, and there is no fear of harm from it if you are careful of the nerve, as we said. And you do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg, exactly. As for the sciatic vein, its location, as we said, is at the heel from the outer side.
And its benefit is for pain in the hip, if it is due to hot blood. And its method of phlebotomy is that you put the patient in the bath, and quickly tie his leg from the hip to above the heel with a four-finger-wide, long, thin bandage, for it does not appear otherwise.
Then, when it appears, phlebotomize it in any way that suits you: either by incision, which is best, or by amputating it, or by splitting it. For its location is safe, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then phlebotomize some of its branches, which appear on the dorsum of the foot towards the little finger and ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you desire.
Then untie the bandage and place cotton on the site of the phlebotomy and bandage the area tightly, for it will quickly heal.
Chapter ninety-six.
On cupping and its method of use.
Cupping instruments may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one is cupping with incision and evacuation of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
This cupping without incision is of two types.
And cupping of the cheeks, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and cupping of the shoulders, and cupping of the coccyx on the rump, and cupping of the forearms, which are the middle of the arms, and cupping of the legs, and cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins spread throughout the flesh, and for that reason it does not weaken as much as bloodletting, and cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any illness that is due to fullness until the entire body is purged.
If cupping is needed due to illness or habit, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle, or at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point where it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their heads, and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck, and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and pain in their eyes, and some itch at the site of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood, and some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, pus, and wounds in their sleep and things like that, so when we see any of that...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water pouring into the eyes.
But it should be after evacuating the whole body, and this cupping can be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein. It is warned against using it for those who are cold-headed or have a cold, for it harms them greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who persist in it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should be instructed to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of generating forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the mastoid processes, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and pain in the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein.  The cupper should be instructed not to deepen his hand with the lancet, lest he cut an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may substitute for phlebotomy of the labial veins. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that arise from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veins, the basilic, cephalic, and median cubital, because it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh and attracts those small veins from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three large veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to deepen the incision, because the area is devoid of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping applied to the coccyx, it benefits from haemorrhoids and lower sores.
The cupper should be instructed that the cupping glass be large and made of copper, because the area requires strong suction and a glass cupping glass might break and require many incisions. As for cupping the legs, it noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, promotes menstruation, benefits from boils and abscesses, and acts in place of phlebotomy of the saphenous veins, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people.  The benefit of cupping the ankles is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied. Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may flow to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then make an incision and gently resume suction. Then observe the condition of the bodies. If a person has loose flesh with porous skin, then one incision should suffice, lest the area ulcerate.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision slightly and deepen it, and to adjust the suction gently and delicately. If the blood is thick, then two incisions should be made; the first to open a path for the thin and watery blood, and the second to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, repeat the incision a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, one incision suffices; if we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we judge the blood to be thick.
Then a deep incision should be made; the moderate limit for the incision is the depth of the skin only. What ointments, waters, and things should be avoided when applying cupping. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
the areas for cupping should be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving ointments. If it is summer, then use rose ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is winter, then use narcissus ointment, iris ointment, chamomile ointment, lily ointment, or similar. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then use marjoram oil, balm oil, frankincense oil, or rue oil, or similar.
If the patient has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These people should wash their cupping sites after cupping with rose water, cold water, gooseberry water, pumpkin water, or chickweed water, or similar. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping sites with vinegar, or with water of the acacia and sumac, or similar. As for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue or chamomile, or similar. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after bathing.
Rather, it should be done an hour or two after leaving the bathhouse. No one should sleep after cupping. What the cupper and phlebotomist should arrange before and after cupping.
First, you must observe whether the patient or phlebotomist is choleric, and whether his blood is predominantly sharp and inflamed.  If so, he should take cooling agents such as pomegranates, dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sherbet, or similar. He should eat the intestines and flesh of sheep, stews, and sour foods, or similar.
And if his temperament is cold, he should be given honeyed drinks, or a drink of quince, or seed oxymel.  He should consume moderately aromatic wine, something between old and new. He should be instructed to eat sparingly, and his food should consist of intestines, chicken giblets, sparrows, and pigeon squabs, stewed. The drink on the day of cupping and phlebotomy should be more than the food.
It may also be necessary to give some people, at certain times, some of the Mithridatium, or musk medicine, or theriac before or after cupping and phlebotomy, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to those suffering from fever.
As for cuppings that are without a condition, these are the cuppings that are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, the region of the kidneys, and the hip joint, because these organs do not tolerate incision, but rather what is sought from them is either drawing blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or using it to draw out cold wind that has settled in an organ, as when we place the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel; it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes pain by resolving that wind.
It may also be placed on the kidneys if there is an obstruction or stone there; by the strength of its drawing, it may perhaps open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place.  It acts similarly when placed on the liver and spleen in cases of wind causing disorder there. These cuppings may be used empty, only by suction.
It may be used with fire, or it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation; that is to say, fill the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the place, held, removed, and repeated several times as needed.  This is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have depicted, and its depth half a span.  There should be a small hole in its side, about halfway up, large enough for a needle to enter; it is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick rim.
Smooth, even, and polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it.  In its center there is a transverse groove of copper or iron where the lighted candle is placed. This cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the illness.
And the instrument's use: for the cupping glasses of boys and thin people are not the same as those of men and corpulent bodies. As for the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the member, it is to place a lighted wick of tightly-spun linen or a small candle of pitch on the middle of the solidified rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the fire will ascend upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest it burn the patient's body. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole we mentioned, until, when we have held the cupping glass as long as needed, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere in place. Then you place the wick in the manner described, and repeat if needed. As for the cupping glass used in the treatment of the shosha with water, it has neither a solidified rod nor a hole.
It is used only by being filled with water and placed on the member. And this is its image:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold more water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of the small size of the member, such as the lip and gum and the like, or because the member is devoid of flesh, such as the finger and nose and the like.
And the method of using them is that you should choose leeches that are in fresh, pure water, free from impurities, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they become hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
until it reddens, then it is placed upon it; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it.  If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off and it is a trickle, then wet a linen cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle upon it powdered alum or gallnut or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply halves of peeled lupins to the place.
And leave it until the lupin adheres to the place, for then the blood will stop.  And it is necessary that if it is necessary to reapply leeches, then do not apply those same leeches if others are available. And if the leeches refuse to adhere, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply them; for when they sense some blood, they will adhere to the place. And if you want to make them fall off, sprinkle upon them some aloes or salt or ash, for then they will fall off immediately.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also contains what is needed in the craft of medicine, which is the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never studied the books of the ancients or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered anyone skilled in it at all. And I have only gained what I have gained from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of that from them, then I devoted myself to experience and practice for a long time.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has followed it, after I have presented it to you, and freed it from the complexities of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you in it many images of the instruments which are used in it.
For it is from an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Most Great.
Chapter One.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them. And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter a summary of the discourse and sections. It compels you first to understand them and to stand upon their truth, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bloodletting or purging, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one to whom this has occurred is a child or a very old man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his diet to cold vegetables and poultry and game, and forbid him drink and thick meats and overeating and every food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling is safe and the flow of material to the place is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.  When the broken bone begins to knit, the patient should be nourished with foods that nourish strongly, thickly, and firmly, containing viscosity such as porridges, rice, heads, and necks, and cow tripe. And eggs and soft fish and thick drinks and the like, for by this regimen the setting of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and join in their first nature.
Because of the dryness of their bones and their hardness. And what was of the bones in the extreme of softness, like the bones of small children, may unite and join. But nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick in it, that adheres to it and strengthens it until one part joins another.
And it binds one part to another until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, so that nothing hinders it in its actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient foods which contain firmness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. And know that fractures differ in their types according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg differs from a fracture of the bone of the head, and a fracture of the bone of the chest differs from a fracture of the bone of the back.
And so all the other members, each differs from another. And we shall come to the mention of each type of fracture explained in its chapter, detailed from the others.  And the type of fracture of the bone may also differ in itself.
For its fracture may be a shattering without splinters occurring in it, and its fracture may be along the length of the bone and its fracture may have splinters and protruding parts, detached or not detached, and the fracture may be with a wound and a perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And among the things by which a fracture of the bone is recognized are its crookedness, its swelling, its appearance to the touch, and its roughness when you probe it with your hand.
Then when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor roughness, nor do you feel a disturbance in the bone when you touch it, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain, or a slight fracture, or a slight crack, then it is not necessary to move it by pulling and probing at all.
But apply to it from the medicines, the mention of which will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is shattered and broken into two without splinters occurring in it, then each part has only separated from its fellow.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to straighten and align it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for days until the hot swelling subsides, then align it in any way you can with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and alignment is easier than a bone in which splinters have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned. If there are splinters in the bone, then it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let the placing of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape, until when the broken part of the bone is extended, then return those protrusions to their places in any way you can with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your might not to cause the patient pain or suffering by your actions, and strive with all your might to unite one bone with its fellow in the best manner. At that time, it is necessary to touch and feel them with your hand. If you see something out of place, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their actions a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have witnessed many times from their actions.
Then, after the alignment, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and calm, and warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, and when turning over and restless, and when defecating, and in all his movements to the best of his ability, and let him strive to keep the limb in a position that ensures against pain.
Because if he feels pain or discomfort in the position of the limb, he should move it to a different position where he does not feel pain, and he should strive to keep that position level and straight.
Lest a deformity occur in the limb if it heals, and as for the method of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you; know that broken limbs differ in their size, shape, and form.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and so on...
then the bandages should be soft and gentle; and those that are large, like the thigh, back, and chest, then the bandages should be wide and firm, for a wide bandage is necessary for a large limb, and it binds it from every side with equal tightness, without any defect.
Then, after you have finished the alignment, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. on a soft splint at the site of the fracture; then begin to wrap the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the limb requires, and pull the bandage slightly with your hand, then take it to the upper part of the fracture.
with less tightness than you applied to the fractured area; then gradually spread the bandage away from the fracture site, loosening the tension little by little until you have obtained a suitable amount of the correct position, then take another bandage and wrap it also around the fractured area.
bandages; then take the bandage to the lower part of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the bandage be as we mentioned in the first upper bandage; then place between the bandages some of the soft splint or cloths to even out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it; then wrap another bandage around it, then apply the firm splints to these bandages immediately, if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply something that will calm that inflammation and reduce the swelling, and leave it for a few days, then apply the splints at that time; and let the splints be of broad, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be of sieve wood.
which are made of pine or palm leaflets or reeds or boxwood and the like of what is available, and let the width of each splint be in this manner and in this exact shape.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what befits the broken limb, in its size and smallness,  Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage, in the same way as your first tightening, then tie from above with firm threads, according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening should be greater at the place of the fracture. And the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, this is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you will not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger's breadth between splint and splint.
If the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints after tightening in the healthy areas, place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that. As for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture occurs in the skin, we will mention that separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that is that if the limb is large, splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, according to your assurance that hot swelling will not occur.
And this is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains; a description of a general, brief bandage that binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is not dominated by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the best part of the flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the mill, and knead it as it is without sifting it with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
The description of another bandage that sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  Take of mastic, leadwort, acacia,  resin, gum tragacanth, and sugar, ten drachms of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and of  athi, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything and mix it with the water of athl or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage, for it is well-composed, quickly mends broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The description of another bandage which is also useful for fractures and sprains: Take gum tragacanth, mastic, and white lead, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms.  Pound all of these very finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white, and apply it.
The description of a bandage for joints and bones that have deviated from their places, and it alleviates the pain that affects them, and for bone cracks and fractures: Take bleached wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil and place it on the affected area. This bandage does not have bone-setting power.
But it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pain in particular. The description of another bandage which sets broken bones: take the leaves of the deaf fig and the leaves of wild poppy, pound them together, and apply them moist. The description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is set.
And you want to resolve the remaining swelling, take of the root of white lead, chamomile, violet flowers, and wheat flour, one part of each. Pound everything together and knead it with white of egg if the member is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or with water, and apply it.
The description of another bandage stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs at the setting of a bone: Take of the root of white lead, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead it with vinegar water, or with fresh water, or with egg white, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its fever.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being moderate between thickness and thinness. As for Galen, he saw that things which contain dryness with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and the like which we mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
The description of a bandage which benefits weakness and pain: Take equal parts of alum, chickpea flour, human hair that is clipped or bird feathers, white lead and salt. Pound, sift, and knead it, and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain...
Then it is loosened; it is that you look; if there is no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place in the affected area, do not loosen it for many days.  If there is severe itching or worrying pain, or swelling in the area.
Then hasten to loosen it at once, and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the itching and pain subside, then let the member rest for an hour.
Then apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night, until you are safe from hot swelling, and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as you did the first time.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely gone, and you need a bandage and tightening, then restore it as you did at first, equally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the limb.
Then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you of the state of the limb, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh is about to knit over it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the nourishing food of the patient beyond what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried and withered more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let that be every three days, and loosen the tightening a little. For by this action, nourishment flows to the limb and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the limb again.
If it is not set properly at first, and is set crookedly, then that is a mistake in their action and a great deception. If it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and it may either sever the bone completely until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called "axe-like".  Alternatively, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating completely. This type of fracture is called a simple fracture. The wound in these two types of fracture may be either large or small.
And there are fractures which are comminuted or contused; their cause being a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two types of fracture may also be either large or small. And there are fractures which are hidden in the bone, fine as a hair, being a slight fissure.
Therefore this type of fracture is called hairline. And there is a fracture caused by a fall or a blow with a stone or the like, which drives a part of the bone inwards, causing a depression at the site, just as happens to a copper pot when struck, a portion of it being driven inwards.
This occurs most often in the soft bones of the head, such as the heads of children. All these types of fracture may have loose and non-loose fragments. We shall describe the treatment of all of them in their proper place. We recognize all these types of fracture by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is recognized by examining the bone, rubbing it and smearing it with ink; the fracture then appears black. As for the treatment of fracture, first look to the symptoms of the patient.
If you see among his symptoms those which clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, protrusion of the eyes and redness, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then begin his treatment. And if the wounded man comes to you immediately after his injury and it is in the winter.
Then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case.  And if it is in the summer, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone spoils and those symptoms which we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is shattering and bruising, then you should cut away the shattered and bruised part as I describe to you.  This is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when you uncover the bone, you see bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat it as necessary.
This is to pack the area with cloths soaked in a syrup and rose oil, and to dress the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to scrape and remove the bone; this can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a fine saw with a narrow blade.  And this is its image:
Then use another saw, slightly wider than the first. And this is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have several saws of different widths, some wider and some shorter than others, and that they be extremely sharp at their edges, and let them be of Indian iron or good steel.
And use gentleness in working the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is necessary that you perforate around it before using the saws, with the drills which are called non-immersive drills; and they are called non-immersive drills.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the cranial bone to what is beyond it, because the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a ring or small circle, preventing it from penetrating and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for each cranium a drill whose sharp point is in length and shortness according to the thickness of that cranium. And this is an image of three types. Of the drills: large, medium, and small.
And as for the method of perforation around the fractured bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then you move the drill to another place, and you make the distance between each hole the size of the thickness of the awl or similar.
Then cut with the saw between each two holes, and do this with the utmost gentleness as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with something else of the instruments which you prepared for that.
Such as forceps and fine hooks. And it is necessary that you be very careful that the drill or saw does not touch anything of the peritoneum.  Then if you have removed the bone and it is free from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and you have taken it out, then it is necessary to cleanse.
And smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another instrument similar to the saw, except that it should be thinner and finer than the other saws.  If anything remains of the small bones and splinters, then take them gently with what you have of the instruments.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with cauterization and ointments which will be mentioned. And as for the other method of this operation, it is a very easy method, far from danger, which Galen mentioned and greatly praised. And this is his statement: First you should begin by uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place under it the end of this lenticular saw, and this is its picture:
