Its lenticular part should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and its sharp part should be on its sides extending lengthwise as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutting edge is against the bone, then you strike the saw from one side.
with a small hammer until you cut through all the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane, so that nothing frightening happens to it at all, even if the surgeon were the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular saw itself and carefully free it from it, for it will be freed from it without injury or fear.
But if the bone fracture did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken of the bone was its surface and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then it is necessary to remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, of which various sizes have been made so that you can use in each place that which is most suitable and best according to what the work requires.
And the same broken bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you use first in scraping the bone the widest of those forceps, then use after it a finer one, and continue to do that successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like cracks and slight fractures, it is necessary to use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing, and this is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the slightest experience in this craft and has studied what we have written and explained in large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can so that you do not burden the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are safe from the hot swelling, then untie it and use afterwards medicines with a drying quality, such as iris roots, crushed costus, crushed myrrh, zedoary, and the like; and that is to make powders from these medicines.
And sprinkle it on the wound as it is dry; and in general, use in that a medicine that cleanses and does not sting, and strive in your treatment of the wound that it be clean, that there be no rancid fat or dirt in it, and do not leave the pus to gather in it at all.
Because if pus gathers on the membrane of the brain, it spoils and rots it, and from that happens to the patient a great calamity; and it may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if one neglects its treatment.
Blackness on its surface is observed; if the patient exhibits the symptoms that we have mentioned, then know that he is inevitably doomed; and if the blackness occurred only from a medicine that was placed on it, and it was in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness.
It is necessary that you take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil and beat them well and smear them on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the various treatments that are necessary until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a broken nose.
Know that only the upper two parts of the nose break, or one of them, because they are bone, for the lower part of it is cartilage and does not break; rather, contusion, crookedness, and flattening occur to it.
If one of its parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert your little finger into the hole of the nose and straighten that fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done by you gently and carefully, so that your action does not cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then it should be straightened with the end of a probe of slight thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, then do likewise, and let him hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible; otherwise, after the seventh or tenth day, when the hot swelling subsides, then insert into the nostril a tent of linen rags if the fracture is on one side, or insert two tents if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the tents be of a thickness such that they fill the nostril.  Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the tents should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not see that this is so; rather, the tents should be moistened in egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the tents until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, after having been wrapped with soft rags, may be inserted into the nose in place of the tents; this will make the retention of the fractured nose firmer and prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; if you wish, you may do it, and if you wish, you may make tents. If a hot swelling occurs in the nose during your work, apply a compress to the nose with kypros or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or something of diachylon ointment.
If a hot swelling does not occur, you should dress it externally with fine semolina and fine frankincense mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it, and do not tie the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured slightly.
Or if they are shattered, you should incise them and remove them with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments suitable for healing and closing wounds.  If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with tents and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On setting the lower jaw if it is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and has not broken into two and is not concave inwards, then its identification is easy.
Then, if the fracture is in the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand, and gently raise the prominence of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you should control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has broken into two, then it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until you are able to straighten it. If it has happened to the teeth, it will loosen.
Or if they are separated, then tie what you hope will remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a small splint on the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth on it, and place on the cloth a tight large splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its placement so that it does not come undone, and instruct the patient to be patient and still, and make his food soft liquids. If you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its placement so that it does not come undone, and instruct the patient to be patient and still, and make his food soft liquids. If you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then hasten to untie it on the third day. Then repair what you bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the splint from it, and place a soft poultice on the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not untie it until it heals and the fracture is solidified. Often this fracture solidifies in three weeks. If a hot swelling occurs during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to calm it until that swelling goes away.
But if the fracture is with a wound, look; if a splinter or splinters have become separated from the bone, be gentle in removing those splinters with whatever instrument suits you for removing them. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel to the extent of your need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains of them, stitch the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture is of one of three types: either it is fractured and broken into two without splinters, which is easiest to set;
or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, the procedure is to have two assistants, one to hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck toward the other side.
Then you should set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither protrusion nor deformity. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
The size of the ball should be according to your need.  Extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow should be placed under his shoulder, and the assistant should press his shoulder downwards so that the clavicle, which is deep, rises upwards. Then set the fracture and smooth it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should incise over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, try to cut it with one of the cutting instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bend. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small, and its length also according to what is possible for the work. Let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening that you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are safe from hot inflammation, join the edges of the wound by suturing. If the opening is small or you fear hot inflammation, dress the wound with lint and pledgets according to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation occurs, moisten the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is to apply to the bone a plaster made from mill dust with egg white. And you place the soft splint on it, then place the ball under his armpit if he needs it. Then take a bandage that is very long and its width is a cubit or thereabouts. Then apply a folded pledget over the splint and plaster, then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise. Then wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage which falls on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as you wind it. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his injured armpit, and pass it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening fixes the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the goal of all this is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage. Then have the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm will be raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture will be elevated with the elevation of the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck, and do not untie the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Reduce [the fracture] and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it becomes stronger and firmer; this happens mostly within twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the reduction of a fracture of the shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, its margins fracture.  Whenever a part of it fractures, or it fractures in its middle, this is known only by touch.  Therefore, depending on what the shape of the fracture is, treat it accordingly.
Straighten it and reduce it to its natural shape by every means you can. Then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft tow. Place on top a pad of folded cloth, then place a wide splint of wood over it.
Thin enough to fit the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is a lump in any part of the shoulder, smooth that lump with soft tow until the splint lies evenly, then bind it from above with a long bandage.
Bind it tightly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and straighten the splint if it moves from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days. When this time is complete, unbind it, and you are safe, for it is among the bones that are not feared for their shattering or displacement. If a splinter of bone protrudes and is irritating under the skin, incise over it and extract it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to alleviate the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the reduction of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely occurs; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur.
And sometimes blood is expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone is evident and not hidden from the touch. Its reduction is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders, then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together by the hands from both sides, and gentleness is employed in straightening it in every way that is possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be, then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of thin wood from willow or elm or something similar which is light, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it move, and pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly, then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity dictates its removal due to suppuration occurring in the location, or pain or swelling, then proceed with its removal, and remove the bandage, and treat what has occurred in that location; then reapply the bandage if you see fit to do so. and face, and bind it firmly until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of ribs if they are fractured: Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick portions adjacent to the back, and their ends in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous, and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And set it by straightening the fracture with the fingers in the accessible way until the shape is properly aligned, then bandage and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs that.  If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then severe pain occurs to the patient.
And it sinks like the sinking that occurs to one with a chest wound, because the bone sinks into the diaphragm, and also dyspnea, cough, and expectoration of much blood occur to him, and this is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many stratagems in this.
And some of them said that it is necessary to make the patient's food that which produces flatulence and wind so that the abdomen swells and expands and the fracture is driven outwards, and we dislike this lest it confirms the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred, and if it has occurred, then it increases it and confirms it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the area, then sucked strongly, and this is more similar to the reasoning, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the area due to its weakness, and some of them said that the area should be covered with wool dipped in hot oil.
And it becomes a pad between the ribs until it is full, so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around, then treat the patient with the treatment of the chest wound in terms of food and medicine, if the patient is exhausted by a severe matter that he cannot bear. And the bone was sinking into the diaphragm, a harmful sinking, and we feared for the patient, so it is necessary that we incise the area and uncover the broken rib, then we place under it the instrument which preserves the peritoneum, which was previously described, and we break the bone gently and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and we treat it with ointments until it heals; if a hot inflammation occurs during this, then act quickly, wetting pads in rose oil and placing them on the area, and treat the patient internally with what alleviates the inflammation.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easier for him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On setting the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens to them, and what mostly happens to them is bruising—and similarly the vertebrae of the back also, if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. Then look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle he does not feel it and does not find pain in them, then know that in most cases he will not recover, and he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the pinch and prick in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has been saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.  If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will recover or not.
Then look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces are expelled involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is dying, so do not bother with his treatment. If nothing of this happens to him, the matter is less serious.  The treatment for what has happened is that you aim to reduce the hot swelling.
By placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, applying it three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying compresses, and bind it tightly with a bandage and instruct him to remain still and quiet.
And he should not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain, until he recovers. If a splinter or something that has broken off from the bone has occurred at the time of the injury, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus.
And set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as you are able to achieve the setting, then apply the compress and splint if needed, then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in such a way that they are shattered at their ends and split lengthwise, and incline inwards.  The patient experiences pain in the affected area, and numbness and tingling in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, then set that fracture as best you can, until its shape resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you can set the fracture.  Once you have set it, apply a bandage, and then apply a splint of wood or leather, and bind it tightly.
Do not fear that the fracture will shift or the splint will come loose. Fill the hollows of the hips with padding so that the binding is even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his sound side. If a hot inflammation should arise, …
desist from setting and binding it until the hot inflammation subsides. Apply what will alleviate it, as previously described. Then return to setting and binding it as it should be. If there are splinters in the bone, or some shattering at its ends, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched, but rather smoothed from the outside, as we have said, and the binding should be left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it is fractured, then it is set in one of two ways. One is that you take a smooth, curved piece of wood, of moderate thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is suspended from a high place, and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is attached to the middle of the curve of the wood, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his two hands until he returns the fracture to what is proper.  The other method is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is suspended from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you wish the stretching to be stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the head of the humerus, then it is necessary that the middle of the bandage be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without force, until when the fracture is set as it should be and unites well, then you bandage it if no hot swelling appears. If hot swelling appears, then leave bandaging it until the seventh day and place on it moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it.  The method of bandaging is that you apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then you bind the forearm to the humerus itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the humerus and forearm so that the forearm acts as splints if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something might change, then use splints.
And that is to place on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then you bandage the splints with the bandage that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your binding on the site of the fracture should be tighter.
And the further from the fracture, the less tight the binding. If you see that the placing of the splints and the binding as we said is appropriate at the time of your setting of the limb, then do so.
And if you fear a hot inflammation, leave off the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur at the site an irritation or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb by reason of excessive tightness.  Then correct all this as we mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of that will occur, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and let a cushion filled with wool be placed under the arm, moderately.
And examine at every time of night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disordered or the bandage become loose. Then correct all this with your effort, and let the patient's food be of the kind we have presented, that is, let the food be light at first, until the bone begins to knit.
Then it is necessary to increase his food. For it is the custom of the arm and leg to knit in forty days. Then it is necessary to loosen [the bandage], and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments which are suitable for that. If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called the ulna; one of them is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is situated under the small one from below.  So perhaps the greater ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps they are broken together.
And when the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. And when the lower ulna is broken, its fracture is bad and its healing is difficult. And the worst is if the two bones are broken together. If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
the physician, when setting it, should make its extension easy and gentle until he straightens it. And if the large ulna is broken, then he should make its extension stronger. And if both ulnas are broken, then he should make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be placed extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should sit squarely on his own.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can.  If there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each splinter to its place to the best of your ability. If a splinter appears that is detached and is piercing the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then make an incision on it and extract it in the manner we mentioned previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then it has been given a separate chapter.
Then take the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a compress with qirūṭī prepared with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the compress on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the qirūṭī and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints, and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding on the location of the fracture stronger and tighter, and whenever you move the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding slightly looser, as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the compresses wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we described. And examine the limb and bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be washed with warm water until that itching subsides, and the limb should be left unbound for a night so that it rests, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something similar, then correct all of that to the best of your ability.  And look also, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening, then it should be loosened slightly and left for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
If nothing of what we mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be untied except after twenty days or so. Then suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight, and he should be careful to avoid restless movements, and let him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that depends on the states of the temperaments and the states of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpus of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely suffer fracture, but they frequently suffer contusion.  So, when a fracture or contusion occurs to the hand,
the patient should sit cross-legged, and before him a chair levelly placed.
Then he places his hand upon it extended, then the assistant extends the broken bones and the physician sets them, until when they are well set, then it is necessary to apply a bandage and a splint, if there is not a hot swelling, then a splint is applied over it according to the place.
And it is inserted into a soft rag; and if the fracture is downwards towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball from a rag and order the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then it is tightened with a long rag.
And let the splint be of soft leather so that it conforms to the skin with the bending of the whole hand, and it is tightened as it should be; and if the fracture is outwards, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another splint below in the hand so that the hand is open and straight.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand turns and is interlaced between the fingers with the bandage; and if the fracture affects one of the phalanges of the fingers, if it is the thumb, then let it be set as it should be, then it is bandaged with the hand, and if you wish, you can make for it a small upright splint to support the fracture.
And it does not move; and if the fracture is to the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little or ring finger, then let them be set and bound with the next sound finger, or bind them all together, which is better, or place a small upright splint upon them.
As we said in the case of the thumb, and examine during your setting and afterwards for hot swelling, and counter it as necessary whenever anything of that occurs, as described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone is frequently fractured and is apparent to the touch because it moves forwards and backwards; and its setting is by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his face.
Then each bandage is extended to its side moderately; this if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the head of the thigh, then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage, either of wool or the like.
At the head of the thigh towards the groin so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture; and similarly if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well; then it is necessary to apply the bandage and tightening, if no hot inflammation occurs in the limb; but if inflammation does occur, leave it for some days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide turban twice or three times around the fracture, and what remains is left. Then the leg is twisted until the heel is at the origin of the buttock, and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are above from both sides.
Then wrap around the leg and thigh what remains of the turban. Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint on the leg bone. Then stuff the space between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin with strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns; and the farther the bandage is from the site of the fracture, let your tightening be less, softer, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you had inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it what is next to it of the splints from above; then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and tighten with them also the ends of the splints from the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the tightening on it as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, and the like, occurs in the limb; if any of that occurs, hasten to untie it and correct whatever of that has happened, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding splinter in the bone, it should be straightened if you can; otherwise, make an incision over it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be bound without the leg being included in the splints.
As we mentioned in the case of the humerus and forearm, except that its setting in this way does not result in lameness for the patient.  But if it is set alone, without the leg being included, the patient will inevitably be lame.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less; all this depends on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap is rare; however, contusion is frequent. If a fracture does occur, it is either a fissure or a shattering into fragments; and this may occur with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will treat it by aligning the separated fragments with your fingers until they are reunited and joined, as far as accuracy, gentleness and skill permit; then apply a bandage and a circular splint if necessary.
Tighten the bandage appropriately from above; then treat all the conditions we have described for other fractures, such as hot inflammation and the like, by counteracting each symptom with the appropriate remedy, until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula;  they are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg will be displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will be displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, especially lower down, the result is obvious.
Therefore, use traction and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, traction should be gentler and lighter, and you should take care in its treatment.
In treating the leg, there is something more than in treating the arm: after you have set the bones and completed your work, take two splints of pine wood, the kind used in plastering rooms, placed between the cracks in the boards, or made from palm leaves or similar material.
Choose splints of moderate thickness, not thin ones, and let their length equal that of the leg from the knee down. Then wrap each splint with two layers of cloth along its length, and place one splint on each side of the leg.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot, then tie the splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle. With this binding, the leg is prevented from tilting to either side and is well supported. A wooden gutter along the length of the leg may also be used.
It is placed in it to keep it from moving, and this is especially necessary in fractures accompanied by a wound.  Then examine the leg daily and tend to it carefully, watching for swelling, inflammation, or other complications. When any such complication arises, treat it accordingly until healing occurs. A leg fracture heals in about thirty days.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the leg bones and toes.
As for the heel, it is never fractured; as for the leg bones, they may be fractured, and the toes are also rarely fractured, but more often bruised.
If a leg bone is fractured and you see that the bones have overlapped, let the patient stand with his foot on the ground as if walking, then you place your foot on the elevated part of the bones, and then tread on them and straighten them.
Until they return to their places; then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot; then firmly bind the foot after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else you need.
If three or four days pass, untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight, whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as you see. As for if some of the toes are fractured, set them and straighten them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe along the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider than it. Then place under the foot the board that I described to you and bind it firmly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured,
make a splint for each toe, appropriately sized, and layered in a soft cloth, and fasten the board to the bottom of the foot, and let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot to ensure a good hold.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures, from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently bend her backward little by little, and let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her pubis is raised little by little; then that cotton comes out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone returns. Then a pad is placed on her back; and when she wants to urinate, the cotton is gently removed so that she may urinate.
And it is returned in the way it was first inserted, then she returns to her first pad. She does this for seven days or so, for it will heal. And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow strongly into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; then the fracture returns. Then pack it with cotton and remain as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it is treated in the same manner.
And its treatment is as we described for the hip bone. The correct treatment of these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone with some experience and understanding of this book, is not hidden, because the fracture itself will in most cases guide you.
Understand the correct way to treat and bind it. As for a fractured penis, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the treatment of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, should immediately be bled if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously explained, are met.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered verdigris if nothing else is available. Then treat the fracture that very same day, do not delay.
If no hot inflammation occurs, then if hot inflammation does occur, delay treatment until the ninth day until the hot inflammation subsides, and do not touch it on the third or fourth day at all, for it will be exposed to bad consequences.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, then you should gently try to reposition and align it with your hands with slight extension. And if you cannot reposition and align it with your hands, then reposition it with this instrument, which is an iron instrument of a length of seven or eight fingers. And its width is according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture.  Let them be round and slightly thick so that they do not bend when pressure is applied during use.
And let the tip be sharp, with a curve at its end, and let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle to the bottom, much thinner.  And this is its image:
And it is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  Then its sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone and pushed with it gently, until, when the bone is returned and somewhat aligned, you complete the alignment of the fractured ends on top of each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it. If you are unable to reset the bone in the way we have described at all, then cut it with a similar cut to those we have mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can. Then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, persistent pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient.  When you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply a poultice or anything greasy to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then, when you have finished setting the bone, apply splints and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And beware, with all caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus. Let your binding be gentle and loose, unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the place, then you should not apply splints. Make bandages from strong cloths in place of the splints and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth you placed on it with the drink, then use lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and the like.  You should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals.  You should position the limb so that the pus flows down easily. If many days pass and the wound does not heal, nor does the pus cease, know that there are small bone fragments.
You should probe the wound. Whatever of those fragments is loose, remove and extract them. Whatever is not loose and is piercing the limb and causing pain, then work at cutting and removing them by whatever means you can.
If the wound shows signs of inflammation, gangrene, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should treat each symptom with a corresponding treatment as previously described in its chapter.
What you should note and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and projects from a limb, such as the thigh bone, humerus, or similar large members, do not attempt to pull or remove it. For death often results from such an attempt. Rather, leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see it is suitable for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications that arise after certain fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture heals, especially those near the joints, making the limb unsightly and perhaps preventing it from its natural function.
Examine it; if the complication is soft, then use astringent medicines such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh,  bdellium, acacia, and the like.  Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication in a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage undisturbed for many days, then unbind it and reapply another until the complication disappears, God willing, or bind a tight lead plate to it.
For lead has the property of removing anything superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has ossified and hardened, and necessity dictates its removal, incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or scrape it off with a scraper until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it has healed but the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has healed, but the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One of them is either due to excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages, or to excessive tightening of the bandages such that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or to excessive and repeated manipulation, or to excessive movement at the wrong time, or to a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases, and to use the bath and to bring him joy and happiness and the like, then to apply pitch to the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or to repeatedly manipulate it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of fractured bones if they are set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or projection of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured. However, if the limb has not been prevented from its natural function, then the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken again should not be accepted; many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country have done this.
And this action is utterly reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is disability; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be treated with water in which emollient herbs such as mallow leaves and root, and rosemary, and the like, have been boiled.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and pounded with chicken fat and butter, and bandaged with it, or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon dung, and similar medicines which are called resolvents.
And sometimes the complication will resolve with constant gentle rubbing by hand, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times should be used; but if the crookedness is old and severe and hardened, and treatment with instruments is necessary.
Then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and what remains of the complication or bone should be cut with fine cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which hinders movement, disfigures the limb, and causes the patient severe pain and suffering.  If a dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it.
One should not delay at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and become inflamed, making reduction of the dislocation difficult. Therefore, one should not delay, nor move, nor extend it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pains.
But if this occurs, one should immediately bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then, bathe the limb with warm water and oil, and then gently reduce it, and treat each limb as described in its place.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as in fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Rarely do both jaws dislocate, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they slightly leave their places and relax a little, or they dislocate completely so that they relax. toward the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech. But if the dislocation is slight, it often returns by itself with the greatest ease.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its reduction quickly and not delay it at all.  This is done by a servant holding the head of the patient, and the physician inserting the thumb of one hand into the origin of the jaw inside his mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from the outside, with which he manipulates it, and he orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every side, and the physician manipulates the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then use fomentations with hot water and oil until their reduction is easy, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said. When they return and become straight and the patient's mouth closes and they do not relax, then apply to them pledgets of cloth with a poultice made of wax, oil, and rose water.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head raised between two pillows, lest he move it to the right or left, and let him not attempt to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is firmly set, then let him eat whatever he desires, and let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is firmly set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they become dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is damaged.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest; it may dislocate outwards, and this is apparent to the touch. Its treatment is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the location with your palm strongly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and splints and bind it. As for its end which is near the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates. If it should dislocate, then it should be reduced and aligned as we have mentioned and as seems appropriate to you.
Then apply a bandage and splints and binding, and instruct the patient to observe rest and stillness until he recovers. With this same treatment, the tip of the shoulder is reduced if it also leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit, downwards; the second is that it dislocates towards the chest; and sometimes it dislocates above the shoulder, but this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the rear because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve.  Most often it dislocates and goes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, for in them it goes out quickly and enters quickly.
But as for those who have much flesh, it is otherwise; that is to say, it goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.  And sometimes a blow or a fall happens to some people, so that the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, and they think that it is dislocated; so it is necessary to examine that until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed with its treatment.  And the dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit: if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the shoulder distorted, and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg.
And the patient is not able to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements. And similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent to the touch, it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lets it down; then it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things which relax and soften, such as cooking the root of mallow, fenugreek and rue in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and firmness; then the physician places his heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, then it returns to its place. And if you wish to reduce it in this way, you have a taller man than the patient stand by his side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is hanging in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down to his belly. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down; then the jaw returns immediately.  And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, its head being round like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin; then it is placed under the patient's armpit, after placing a soft rag on the head of the wood, and the patient standing along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards on the other side, and extend his body also forcefully from the other side; then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is the size of four fingers and its thickness is the size of two fingers; it has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then you tie a soft rag on the round head lest the wood harm the patient; then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbeam, level with the width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side; then the joint enters immediately. If its entry is achieved in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder, as it goes around from above; then the ball is firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wound from above, and his hand is hung from his neck.
And he is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be little so that the limb may strengthen, for this hastens its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised; if it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or some other cause, then cauterization with three points should be used, as described in the chapter on cauterization.  If all this has been done and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not firm,
then the bandage and tightening should be repeated many times; if it is not firm, and it has fallen and relaxed, and you cannot raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the humerus is severed, or stretched, or relaxed; then know that the joint will never be firm in its place.
As for the dislocation which is towards the chest and breast, and to the back, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns; and all the bandaging and treatment are used until it is healed.  If, after healing, numbness appears in the limb and movement is slow,
let the patient use the bath many times until that numbness softens and returns to its former nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty also; and it dislocates to all sides, especially to the front or to the back; and its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is under sight and under touch, to whichever side it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be apparent to you clearly by the hollowing of the joint; and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it; and you should hasten and reduce the dislocation at once before a hot swelling occurs in it.
For if a hot swelling occurs in it, its reduction is difficult, and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is to the back; for that is the worst of all kinds of dislocation and the most painful, and very often death ensues with it.
And its reduction, if it is of a kind that can be reduced, is that a servant extends his hand with both his hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front, it may be reduced by bending the hand once until the base of its palm strikes the shoulder which it faces; and if the dislocation does not return to its place, then use very strong extension, which is that two servants extend the arm.
And two servants also hold the patient so that he does not move during the extension; then the arm is rotated to every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand; and when the physician touches the joint with his hands, he anoints them with oil so that it helps in easily sliding the joint.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns; and after its return, it is necessary to apply a bandage containing astringents and desiccants with egg white, and it is tightly bound, and the arm is suspended from the patient's neck, and it is left for some days.
Then it is untied; and if the joint is firm in its place, then untie the bandage from it and leave it; and if you see that the joint is not yet strengthened, then reapply the bandage and leave it also for some days until it strengthens, then untie it; and if there is swelling after the return of the joint and its unbinding,
and difficulty in movement, then let him use fomentation in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens, or place on the joint the udder of a fat sheep, then bind it and leave it on him for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath; and when he sweats, then massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the udder once, twice, and three times with entering the bath until it softens; and if you wish to apply moistened, light ox-dung with fat and bind it on him, do that repeatedly, for it softens and returns to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often becomes dislocated, and its reduction is easy unlike other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it becomes dislocated before the place becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
And its reduction is that you place the patient's wrist on a board, and an attendant extends his hand, and the physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns; however, it is necessary to look if the dislocation has gone to the inside of the hand.
So let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction, and if the dislocation is protruding to the back of the hand, let the palm of his hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint; if it returns immediately, otherwise, bind it with a bandage that soothes the swelling.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after days have passed, except that the joint remains crooked and does not harm the patient except if the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve has been severed or bruised, and there is no remedy for it except cauterization; perhaps it will benefit, and perhaps it will not benefit at all. When the reduction of the wrist is complete, apply the bandage that we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then it is untied, and the hand is exercised; if its movement is difficult and there is some stiffness in it, soften it with warm water and rubbing repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter twenty-nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction; if a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns, then bind the tip of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day; if it is at night, bind it in the same way, but continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; do this for several days until it strengthens.
And likewise do with it if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand, binding it towards that same direction and doing to it as you did before until it heals; and likewise do with it if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, then there is no treatment, for death quickly overtakes the patient.
And the sign of that is that the patient's stool is passed involuntarily, he is unable to retain it, and often some of his limbs become relaxed, either his legs or his arms, or one of them; and if one vertebra is displaced from its position, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement is in four directions; that which is displaced backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should look; if the hump has occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it and no cure at all. As for that which has occurred from a fall or a blow, and the like…
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are of little benefit. I have shortened this, so that a little of it suffices for much of what they presented, by approximating the meaning and explaining it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
So I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure, and so too that which occurs to the sides; only that which occurs in the back is treated, in the way that I describe, which is to stretch out the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet cloth beneath him so that his chest is not harmed. Then place an upright wooden post, fixed in a hole in the ground at the head of the bench, and another post toward his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the post—let it not be firmly fixed in the hole—and another servant holds the other post in that manner. Then wrap the patient’s chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the post at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his heels. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other post at his feet. Then each servant pulls his post with the bandage, and the two posts do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not firmly fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump forcefully until it returns, or he places a board on it and then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board about three cubits long.
And dig into the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place into which the end of the board fits. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
And it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-twisted screw, then you plant in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two posts, each a cubit long, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two posts.
Let the distance between each post be a cubit. And a hole is made in each post through which the screw passes. And all the posts are firmly fixed in the ground so that they do not move at all. And insert a round stick, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two posts.
And at its end is a hole in which a stick a cubit long is firmly fixed by twisting. And the same is done in the other two posts. Then tighten the bandage which you have tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw at his head, and the bandages which you have tightened on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, twisting his hand with the twister with which he twists the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying bandage with egg white, then tow, then place on top of the bandage a splint of wood whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and its length is the extent of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with using the splint which we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used in this. And sometimes a swelling appears at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and it is an extra bone that has grown.  Do not treat it with this treatment, for death might result.
Chapter thirty-one.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to what affects other joints of slight displacement and depression.  The hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards. It is most often dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards. The sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected leg, the sound leg will be longer.
And the knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the place next to the groin will be noticeably swollen, because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms. And the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking he treads on his heel.  And the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin. As for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look: if the dislocation is old, having persisted with the patient for some time, and no one has attempted to reduce it, and it has remained in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
Then you should not attempt to treat it.  But as for the one whose dislocation is recent, and whatever its type may be of the four forms of dislocation, hasten to twist the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, perhaps...
it will return and will not need other treatment. If it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong, capable assistant who will extend his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who will extend it from above.
by placing his hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage around the root of the thigh, and a third assistant holding the end of the bandage. The extension will be either from the front, from the groin towards the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
And their extension will all be at once until the patient is raised from the ground with his body and remains suspended. This type of extension is common to the four types. If the dislocation returns as we have said, otherwise, each type will require the special treatment I will mention.
As for its special reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the area under the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle. Then another strong, capable assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, so that it returns to its place.
This method is easier than all other methods of treating this condition. If it proves difficult and does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then the patient's legs should be tied together with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
The distance between each [bandage] and its neighbour will be four fingers, and the affected leg will be extended two fingers more than the other. Then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and he will be two cubits from the ground.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient; and the other boy who is embracing the thigh pushes forcefully, so the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its special reduction, if the dislocation is outwards,
the patient should lie on the operating table as we described for the humpbacked patient, and a bandage is tightened around his affected leg in particular and his chest. Then two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, and then an extra piece of wood is placed in the middle of the table, very firmly secured.
A wet rag is wrapped around it so that it does not injure the patient; the wood is placed between his thighs so that he does not slip downwards when pulled. Then each attendant pulls on his side, and the doctor sets the hip with his hands; if it responds to reduction,
otherwise, place the board on him and press down as we mentioned in the case of the humpback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side. If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be extended forcefully while he is in this same position on the operating table.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip and then presses it with the other hand, meanwhile extending the pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be pulled downwards.
He is raised from the ground, but should be placed on something firm, as he should also be if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we mentioned about his lying on the operating table on his face with the bandages tightened.
as we said before, and the pressure with the board should also be applied to the place where the joint came out. When the hip joint is completely reduced as it should be—and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you—you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them even and the patient can flex and extend his leg without difficulty, know that the limb has been reduced as it should be. Then bind the thighs together, apply the bandage and tighten it firmly with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days. Then remove the bandage and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the reduction has been successful and allow the patient to walk. If you see any laxness in it,
reapply the bandage and tighten it as you did before, and leave it for another three days. Then remove it, and he should walk slowly on it for a few days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
In the treatment of the reduction of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, that is, backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all.  The sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. The treatment for all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended, if he is able to do so, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands on the sides of his knee strongly, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly and without force. Then bandage it and bind the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only use little walking for some days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it by what we described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of the fracture of the heel.
The heel may be displaced slightly, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent towards the side to which it dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to return it; this is done by gently pulling it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then it is necessary that you seat the patient sitting, and a strong servant holds him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot from above with your right hand and from below his foot at the place of the heel with your left hand, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then with the left twice, then straighten it with the left and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without violence.
Do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot to the leg on the third time while you pull at the heel; if it returns in one or two times in this manner and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the action on it, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and firmly fix a peg in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not hurt the patient; then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant stretches the leg, either with his hands or with a rope that he ties around the neck of the leg; then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to his companion, and the peg remains between the patient's thighs holding him so that his body does not slip downwards during the stretching, then the physician sets the dislocation with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the dislocation returns quickly; when the dislocation returns and you are certain of its return, apply the bandage and splint and bind it tightly with bandages and bind the foot with a bandage to the bottom, and it is necessary to protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
lest the bandage be too tight and hurt him; then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage loosens, tighten it, then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and the patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the dislocation will not recur and be spoiled and will not accept treatment afterwards; if a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to use in its calming what we have previously described in another place of treatment and application until it disappears.
Chapter thirty-four.
In the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
What is dislocated should be straightened with a slight pull without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking, then you should place your foot on the dislocated joint and then press it down firmly with your foot until it returns and you see that it is straight and no prominence appears in the area.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot which encompasses the whole foot and has two ends; then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and the recurrence is prevented, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When something of that sort happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it frequently results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the art, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, not rash or impetuous, and that he should initially use only medicines which calm hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
For God's sake, except for what you hope will be safe from harm with mildness of the disease and you see in it some hope, then return it from the moment in the first instance before the hot swelling develops.
If the limb returns to what you intended, then use the treatment which calms hot swellings and treat the wound with whatever is suitable for it from drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and splinters are formed in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases, which preceded in their places.
And exert your effort and keep yourself from entering the path of risk, as I have previously advised you, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
